B) It is exclusive of conjunction only.
C) It is exclusive of emptiness only.
D) It is exclusive of neither.

Key Takeaway
A positive disjunctive conditional exclusive of emptiness only permits multiple truths but prohibits all falsehoods; it is defined by the impossibility of emptiness.

Negative Disjunctive Conditionals Exclusive of Emptiness Only
Negative example of disjunctive conditional exclusive of emptiness only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify negative disjunctive conditionals that are exclusive of emptiness only and explain how negation interacts with the exclusivity condition.

Core Idea
A negative disjunctive conditional exclusive of emptiness only negates the claim that at least one alternative must be true.

After illustrating the positive form, the author turns to the negative counterpart. He provides an example from astronomy: 'Not at all either the moon and earth are equal or the moon is greater than the earth.' This negative disjunctive denies that the disjunction is exclusive of emptiness. In reality, the moon is smaller, so neither alternative is true, making the emptiness-exclusivity claim false. By analyzing this, you will understand how negation targets the exclusivity condition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive alternatives
The disjunction in the example is: 'Either the moon and earth are equal, or the moon is greater than the earth.' There are two alternatives.
Note the disjunction does not include the possibility of the moon being smaller.
2
Determine the truth of the alternatives
In reality, the moon is smaller than the earth, so both alternatives are false. The moon is not equal to the earth nor greater.
This means the disjunction, if claimed as exclusive of emptiness, would be false because both are false.
3
Understand the negation's target
The proposition 'Not at all either ... or ...' negates the entire disjunction. The author explains that the negation is directed at the claim of emptiness-exclusivity (that at least one must hold). Since emptiness occurs (both false), the negation is true.
Thus the negative disjunctive conditional is true precisely when the positive disjunctive exclusive of emptiness is false.
4
Classify the type
Because the negation only denies that emptiness is impossible (and does not deny that conjunction is impossible), this is a negative disjunctive conditional that is exclusive of emptiness only (i.e., it negates that exclusivity).
It is important to distinguish this from negating other types of exclusivity.

The Three Pillars of Propositions: Categorical, Conjunctive, Disjunctive
Provides concise definitions of hamliyya, muttasila, and munfasila propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of propositions (hamliyya, muttasila, munfasila) and correctly classify any given proposition from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Categorical predicates; conjunctive connects; disjunctive divides.

In logic, propositions are the building blocks of arguments. This passage from our coursebook gives you the three fundamental types you will encounter in every Islamic science. Mastering these definitions is your first step to analyzing any legal or theological claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of judgment
For categorical propositions, there is a single subject-predicate pair. For conditionals, the judgment is about a relationship between two judgments.
Categorical: e.g., 'Every intoxicant is haram'. Conditional: involves two judgments.
2
Check for conditionality
Categorical has no condition. Conjunctive uses 'if...then'. Disjunctive uses 'either...or' or expresses incompatibility.
The presence of a conditional word (if, either) indicates a conditional proposition.
3
Note the number of relations
Categorical has one relationship. Conjunctive has two judgments with a conditional link. Disjunctive involves two or more relations with alternation or incompatibility.
Disjunctives can be exclusive ('either...or...but not both') or inclusive (at least one).
4
Memorize the Arabic key terms
Learn the three terms: قضية حملية, قضية شرطية متصلة, قضية شرطية منفصلة. Practice writing them and their definitions.
Use the provided translations for reinforcement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خلاصة تعريفات القضايا : القضية الحملية : هي التي يكون الحكم فيها قائماً على إسناد شيء إلى شيء آخر أو نفيه عنه . القضية الشرطية المتصلة : هي التي يكون الحكم فيها قائماً على إثبات الارتباط الشرطي أو نفيه بين حكم وحكم آخر . القضية الشرطية المنفصلة : هي التي يكون الحكم فيها قائماً على الترديد بين نسبتين فأكثر أو على نفي هذا الترديد . أو هي التي يحكم فيها بتنافي نسبتين فأكثر أو عدم تنافيهما . ١٠١
Summary of definitions of propositions: The categorical proposition is that in which the judgment is based on attributing something to something else or negating it from it. The conditional conjunctive proposition is that in which the judgment is based on affirming or negating a conditional connection between one judgment and another. The conditional disjunctive proposition is that in which the judgment is based on a disjunction between two or more relations, or on negating this disjunction. Or it is that in which a judgment is made about two or more relations being incompatible or not incompatible. 101
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a concise summary defining the three main types of propositions in logic. First, the categorical proposition (hamliyya) involves a direct attribution or negation of a predicate to a subject. For example, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' attributes 'forbidden' to 'intoxicant'. Second, the conditional conjunctive proposition (shartiyya muttasila) expresses a conditional connection, such as 'If the sun rises, then it is daytime' — the judgment is about the link between two judgments. Third, the conditional disjunctive proposition (shartiyya munfasila) expresses a disjunction (alternation) between two or more relations, either affirming that at least one holds (e.g., 'Either this act is wajib or it is haram') or negating that disjunction, or it can involve incompatibility or compatibility. The summary provides a clear differentiation for memorization.

Key Terms
قضية حملية
قضية شرطية متصلة
قضية شرطية منفصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The three proposition types—categorical, conjunctive, and disjunctive—form the foundation for all logical reasoning in Islamic sciences.

First Rule of Predication: 'Umum wa Khusus Mutlaq
Author states the first rule of predication for categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the relationship of absolute generality and particularity between two terms and correctly apply the rule of predication to construct true universal affirmative propositions.

Core Idea
When one term is absolutely more general than another, you can affirm it of every instance of the specific, but not vice versa.

In this lesson, we explore the first rule of predication from classical Islamic logic: the relationship of absolute generality and particularity (umum wa khusus mutlaq). This rule governs how we can truthfully predicate a general term of a specific term in categorical propositions. Understanding this rule is essential for constructing sound arguments in Islamic sciences, where clear predication is key.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Look at the examples: 'human' and 'animal', 'apple' and 'fruit', 'milk' and 'white'. Determine which is more general (a'am) and which is more specific (akhass).
General term has wider extension; specific term is included under it.
2
Understand the relationship
The general applies to everything the specific applies to, but not vice versa. For instance, every human is an animal, but not every animal is human.
This is absolute generality (umum mutlaq), not partial overlap.
3
Apply the rule for positive predication
When you predicate the general of all individuals of the specific, you get a true universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every human is an animal'). Check that this follows the rule.
4
Check the limits
Note that you cannot predicate the specific of all individuals of the general (e.g., 'Every animal is human' is false). The specific can only be predicated of some individuals (e.g., 'Some animals are human').
This is why the rule says 'only of some individuals'.
5
Distinguish from other relationships
Contrast with partial overlap (umum wa khusus min wajh) where the relationship is not one-way. For example, 'animal' and 'white' overlap partially, not absolutely.
The type of relationship determines which predications are valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۱)
ضوابط الحمل في القضايا الحملية
بالرجوع إلى مفاهيم النسب في دائرة الألفاظ والمعاني، تنكشف لنا مجموعة من الحقائق، نستبين منها ضوابط الحمل في القضايا الحملية.
أولاً: ما بينهما عموم وخصوص مطلق من المفردات التصورية يحمل الأعم منهما على كل أفراد الأخص حمل إيجاب لا حمل سلب، ويحمل الأخص منهما على بعض أفراد الأعم فقط حمل إيجاب وحمل سلب (ولا يحمل على كل أفراد الأعم).
أمثلة من حمل الأعم على الأخص:
الأخص نجعله موضوعاً الأعم نجعله محمولاً
كل إنسان حيوان
كل تفاح فاكهة
كل لبن أبيض
من هذه الأمثلة نلاحظ أننا استطعنا أن نحمل - ونحن صادقون - الأعم منها، وهو (حيوان - فاكهة - أبيض) على كل أفراد الأخص منها، وهو (إنسان - تفاح - لبن) حمل إيجاب، ولا يجوز حمل السلب لأن القضية تكذب حينئذ، فإن أردنا قضية كاذبة فلنحمل.
(11)
Rules of predication in categorical propositions
By referring to the concepts of relations in the sphere of words and meanings, a set of facts is revealed to us, from which we discern the rules of predication in categorical propositions.
First: [between] what is between them absolute generality and particularity among conceptual terms: the more general of them is predicated of every individual of the more specific with positive predication, not negative predication. And the more specific of them is predicated only of some individuals of the more general with positive predication and negative predication (and it is not predicated of every individual of the more general).
Examples of predicating the more general of the more specific:
We make the more specific the subject and the more general the predicate:
Every human is an animal.
Every apple is a fruit.
Every milk is white.
From these examples we observe that we were able to predicate - while being truthful - the more general of them, which is (animal, fruit, white) of every individual of the more specific, which is (human, apple, milk) with positive predication. And negative predication is not permissible because the proposition would then be false. So if we want a false proposition, let us predicate...
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage introduces the first rule of predication (haml) in categorical propositions, focusing on the relationship of absolute generality and particularity (umum wa khusus mutlaq). This relationship holds when one term (the general) encompasses all individuals of the other term (the specific), such as 'animal' and 'human'. The rule states: (1) The more general term (a'am) can be predicated positively of every individual of the more specific (akhass). For example, 'Every human is an animal' is true. (2) The more specific term can be predicated positively of some individuals of the more general (e.g., 'Some animals are human') and negatively of some individuals (e.g., 'Some animals are not human'), but it cannot be predicated positively of all individuals of the more general (e.g., 'Every animal is human' is false). The author provides examples: 'Every human is an animal', 'Every apple is a fruit', 'Every milk is white'. These show the general predicated positively of all specifics. The author notes that negative predication would yield false propositions here (e.g., 'No human is an animal' is false). The text ends with an incomplete remark about false propositions, but the rule is fully stated.

Key Terms
عموم وخصوص مطلق
حمل إيجاب
حمل سلب
أعم
أخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following correctly follows the rule of absolute generality and particularity (umum wa khusus mutlaq)?
A) Every animal is human.
B) Every human is animal.
C) No human is animal.
D) Some human is animal.

Key Takeaway
The rule of umum wa khusus mutlaq states that the more general term can be predicated affirmatively of every individual of the more specific, but the specific term can only be predicated of some individuals of the general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Partial Predication: Using 'Some' in Logical Statements
Explains partial predication and gives examples of general-specific and specific-general predications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a partial predication and explain why it does not imply anything about the remainder of the subject.

Core Idea
Partial predication tells you about a part; the rest stays unspoken, not false.

In logic, not every statement needs to cover all individuals. Sometimes a speaker only intends a part. This lesson explains what 'partial predication' (al-haml al-juz'i) means and how to handle it correctly. You will see examples from the text and learn to avoid the common mistake of assuming the converse.

Guided Path
1
Understand the speaker's license
The passage states that a speaker may limit himself to a partial predication for a purpose, and is not obliged to state all he knows. This is the key justification for using 'some' statements.
2
Examine the examples given
Look at the examples: 'Some human is animal', 'Some apple is fruit', 'Some milk is white'. Notice the use of 'some' (ba'd). The subject is partial, not universal.
3
Note the non-implication
The text explicitly says that from 'Some A is B' we do not infer that the rest of A is not B. The rest is simply unspoken and not judged upon.
This avoids the fallacy of illicit subalternation.
4
Practice reversing the order
The passage also shows predicating the specific on the general: 'Some animal is human', 'Some fruit is apple', etc. Again, it's a partial predication true for some individuals.
5
Recognize both affirmative and negative forms
The same structure works for negative as well: 'Some animal is not human'. The part affirmed is different from the part negated, so both can be true simultaneously.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلِلْمُتَكَلِّمِ أَنْ يَقْتَصِرَ عَلَى الْحَمْلِ الْجُزْئِيِّ لِغَرَضٍ يَهْدِفُ إِلَيْهِ فِي كَلامِهِ، وَيَظَلُّ كَلامُهُ صَحِيحاً صَادِقاً، لأَنَّ الْمُتَكَلِّمَ غَيْرُ مُلْزَمٍ بِبَيَانِ جَمِيعِ مَا يَعْلَمُ عَنِ الْقَضِيَّةِ، هَذَا إِذَا كَانَ عَالِماً بِكُلِّ عَنَاصِرِ الْقَضِيَّةِ، فَإِنْ كَانَ جَاهِلاً بِبَعْضِ عَنَاصِرِ الْقَضِيَّةِ عَالِماً بِبَعْضِهَا، كَانَ الاِقْتِصَارُ عَلَى الْحَمْلِ الْجُزْئِيِّ الْمَعْلُومِ لَدَيْهِ هُوَ الأَمْرُ الْوَاجِبُ عَلَيْهِ. وَحِينَ يَقْتَصِرُ عَلَى الْحَمْلِ الْجُزْئِيِّ يَقُولُ: (بَعْضُ الإِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانٌ - بَعْضُ التُّفَّاحِ فَاكِهَةٌ - بَعْضُ اللَّبَنِ أَبْيَضُ) وَلا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ هَذَا مَفْهُومٌ مُخَالِفٌ أَنَّ الْبَعْضَ الآخَرَ لَيْسَ كَذَلِكَ، وَإِنَّمَا يَبْقَى الْبَعْضُ الآخَرُ مَسْكُوتاً عَنْهُ تَمَاماً، غَيْرَ مَحْكُومٍ عَلَيْهِ بِشَيْءٍ. أَمْثِلَةٌ مِنْ حَمْلِ الأَخَصِّ عَلَى الأَعَمِّ: نَعْكِسُ الأَمْثِلَةَ السَّابِقَةَ عَلَى الْوَجْهِ التَّالِي فَنَحْمِلُ الأَخَصَّ عَلَى الأَعَمِّ، فَنَقُولُ: الأَعَمَّ نَجْعَلُهُ مَوْضُوعاً الأَخَصَّ نَجْعَلُهُ مَحْمُولاً. بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ - بَعْضُ الْفَاكِهَةِ تُفَّاحٌ - بَعْضُ الأَبْيَضِ لَبَنٌ. وَمِنْ هَذِهِ الأَمْثِلَةِ نَلاحِظُ أَنَّنَا اسْتَطَعْنَا أَنْ نَحْمِلَ - وَنَحْنُ صَادِقُونَ - الأَخَصَّ مِنْهَا وَهُوَ (إِنْسَانٌ - تُفَّاحٌ - لَبَنٌ) عَلَى بَعْضِ أَفْرَادِ الأَعَمِّ مِنْهَا وَهُوَ (حَيَوَانٌ - فَاكِهَةٌ - أَبْيَضُ) حَمْلَ إِيجَابٍ كَمَا سَبَقَ، وَنَسْتَطِيعُ أَيْضاً أَنْ نَحْمِلَ الأَخَصَّ عَلَى الأَعَمِّ حَمْلَ سَلْبٍ فَنَقُولُ: (بَعْضُ الْحَيَوَانِ لَيْسَ بِإِنْسَانٍ - بَعْضُ الْفَاكِهَةِ لَيْسَ بِتُفَّاحٍ - بَعْضُ الأَبْيَضِ لَيْسَ بِلَبَنٍ) وَيَظَلُّ الْكَلامُ صَادِقاً صَحِيحاً، لأَنَّ الْبَعْضَ الَّذِي حَمَلْنَا عَلَيْهِ الأَخَصَّ حَمْلَ إِيجَابٍ غَيْرُ الْبَعْضِ الَّذِي حَمَلْنَا عَلَيْهِ الأَخَصَّ حَمْلَ سَلْبٍ.
And the speaker may limit himself to the partial predication for a purpose he aims at in his speech, and his speech remains correct and truthful, because the speaker is not obliged to state everything he knows about the proposition. This is if he knows all the elements of the proposition. But if he is ignorant of some elements of the proposition while knowing others, then limiting himself to the partial predication which he knows is what is obligatory upon him. And when he limits himself to the partial predication, he says: 'Some human is animal - some apple is fruit - some milk is white', and from this it is not understood that the other part is not so; rather the other part remains completely unspoken of, not judged about anything. Examples of predicating the more specific on the more general: We reverse the previous examples in the following way and predicate the more specific on the more general, saying: We make the more general the subject and the more specific the predicate: Some animal is human - some fruit is apple - some white is milk. And from these examples we notice that we were able - while being truthful - to predicate the more specific, which is (human, apple, milk), on some individuals of the more general, which is (animal, fruit, white), as affirmative predication as before. And we can also predicate the more specific on the more general as negative predication, saying: 'Some animal is not human - some fruit is not apple - some white is not milk', and the speech remains truthful and correct, because the part upon which we predicated the more specific affirmatively is different from the part upon which we predicated the more specific negatively.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the concept of partial predication (al-haml al-juz'i). It first explains that a speaker may use a partial (particular) predication for a specific purpose, and doing so does not make the statement false because the speaker is not obligated to mention everything he knows. If the speaker knows some elements but not others, it is obligatory to restrict the predication to what is known. The examples given are 'Some human is animal', 'Some apple is fruit', 'Some milk is white'. Importantly, such a statement does not imply that the other part is false; it simply remains unmentioned. Then the passage gives examples of predicating the more specific (al-akhass) on the more general (al-a'am), reversing the earlier examples. In these examples, we make the more general the subject and the more specific the predicate, e.g., 'Some animal is human'. Both affirmative and negative forms are valid, as long as the part referred to in the affirmative is different from that in the negative. The key point is that partial predication does not distribute either term fully; it only applies to a part.

Key Terms
الحمل الجزئي
الأخص
الأعم
حمل إيجاب
حمل سلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a speaker says 'Some human is animal', what can we correctly infer?
A) The rest of human is not animal.
B) The rest of human is animal.
C) The rest of human is not spoken about.
D) All human is animal.

Key Takeaway
Partial predication ('some A is B') does not indicate anything about the rest of A; the remainder is simply not addressed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following is a partial predication: 'All scholars are knowledgeable', 'Some gold is yellow', 'No philosopher is irrational'. For each, explain why or why not.
?
Consider the statement 'Some action is obligatory'. According to the rule of partial predication, what can you correctly infer about the rest of the actions? Write a brief explanation.

Defining the Overlap Relation: General and Specific from One Aspect
Author defines the second logical relation and its distribution rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the overlap relation between two terms and determine which categorical propositions are true in that relation.

Core Idea
Overlap means each term has exclusive members; only particular propositions are true.

In Islamic logic, terms relate in four ways. The second, 'عموم وخصوص من وجه' (overlap), is crucial for evaluating theological and legal propositions. The author uses animal/white, tall/speaking, beautiful/human to illustrate that in such relations, only 'some' statements are true.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Locate the subject and predicate in the proposition. For example, 'animal' and 'white' in the passage.
2
Check the overlap conditions
Ask: Are there individuals that belong to the first term but not the second? Are there individuals that belong to the second but not the first? Are there individuals that belong to both? If all three are true, the relation is overlap.
3
Apply the rule for universals
In overlap, universal affirmative ('Every A is B') and universal negative ('No A is B') are false because of the exclusive and shared members.
4
Determine valid propositions
Only particular affirmative ('Some A is B') and particular negative ('Some A is not B') are true. The author shows this with examples like 'Some animal is white' and 'Some animal is not white'.
5
Check distribution
Neither term can be distributed (taken universally) in a true proposition. If distributed, the proposition becomes false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عموم وخصوص من وجه، أي بين كل فرد وصاحبه عموم من جهة وخصوص من جهة أخرى، وباستطاعتنا أن نقول فيها :
موضوع محمول موضوع محمول
بعض الحيوان أبيض بعض الحيوان ليس أبيض
بعض الأبيض حيوان بعض الأبيض ليس حيواناً
بعض الطويل متكلم بعض الطويل ليس متكلماً
بعض المتكلم طويل بعض المتكلم ليس طويلاً
بعض الجميل إنسان بعض الجميل ليس إنساناً
بعض الإنسان جميل بعض الإنسان ليس جميلاً
ولا يقال فيها إيجاباً : (كل حيوان أبيض، أو كل أبيض حيوان) وهكذا في كل الأمثلة .
ولا يقال فيها سلباً : (لا شيء من الحيوان أبيض، أو لا شيء من الأبيض بحيوان) وهكذا في كل الأمثلة .
فإن قيل شيء من ذلك كانت القضية كاذبة .
نظرة ثانية حول الاستغراق :
ونلاحظ هنا أنه لا يصح في قضايا موضوعها ومحمولها بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه أن يكون أيّ منهما مستغرقاً لجميع أفراده، والمقال فيها يتناول بعض أفرادهما فقط، ولا يصح غير ذلك لا في السلب ولا في الإيجاب، وإلا كذبت القضية نظراً إلى الواقع .
General and specific from one aspect, meaning between each individual and its counterpart there is generality from one respect and specificity from another respect. And we can say about it:
Subject Predicate Subject Predicate
Some animal is white Some animal is not white
Some white is animal Some white is not animal
Some tall is speaking Some tall is not speaking
Some speaking is tall Some speaking is not tall
Some beautiful is human Some beautiful is not human
Some human is beautiful Some human is not beautiful
And one does not say in affirmation: (Every animal is white, or every white is animal) and likewise in all the examples.
And one does not say in negation: (No animal is white, or no white is animal) and likewise in all the examples.
For if something like that is said, the proposition would be false.
A second observation about distribution:
And we note here that in propositions whose subject and predicate have a relationship of general and specific from one aspect, it is not valid for either of them to be distributed over all its individuals; the discourse about them concerns only some of their individuals. And nothing else is valid, neither in negation nor in affirmation, otherwise the proposition would be false with respect to reality.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage defines the second type of logical relation between two terms: 'عموم وخصوص من وجه' (general and specific from one aspect). In this relation, each term applies to some individuals that the other does not, and they also overlap in some individuals. The author illustrates with examples from animal/white, tall/speaking, beautiful/human, and their negations. The key rule is that in such propositions, one cannot assert a universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every animal is white') or a universal negative (e.g., 'No animal is white') because both would be false given the actual overlap and difference. The observation about distribution ('استغراق') states that neither term can be distributed across all its individuals; only partial (particular) propositions are valid. This is because, for example, if one said 'Every animal is white,' it would be false since some animals are not white. Similarly, 'No animal is white' would be false since some animals are white. The same applies to the predicate if it were universal. Thus, only particular affirmative and particular negative propositions are allowed in this relation, as shown in the examples.

Key Terms
عموم وخصوص من وجه
استغراق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the relation between 'animal' and 'white', which proposition is true according to the passage?
A) Every animal is white
B) No animal is white
C) Some animal is white
D) Some animal is not white

Key Takeaway
In the overlap relation, only particular propositions are true; universal propositions are false because of the combination of shared and exclusive members.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the terms 'speaking' (متكلم) and 'tall' (طويل) from the passage. List one example of an individual that is speaking but not tall, one that is tall but not speaking, and one that is both. Then write a true particular proposition and a false universal proposition about them.
?

The Disjoint Relation (Tabaayun) - Definition and Distribution Rules
Author defines the third logical relation and its distribution rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify disjoint terms in any pair and determine the correct negative predication and distribution pattern.

Core Idea
Disjoint terms share nothing — not a single individual — so only negative predication ever holds.

This passage introduces the third logical relation between terms: complete disjointness (tabaayun). Unlike intersection (al-umum wa al-khusus) where some overlap exists, disjoint terms have zero common individuals. The author explains that only negative propositions are possible, and then examines the distribution consequences in universal and particular negatives. Understanding this helps you evaluate statements about contradictory or contrary pairs in Islamic theology and law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of tabaayun
Find the phrase 'ما بينهما تباين' and read the description: two terms differ completely in meaning (al-ma'na) and extension (al-masdaq).
2
Recognize the subtypes included
Note that the author explicitly includes al-mutadaddan (contraries) and al-mutanaqidan (contradictories) under this relation.
This broadens the scope of tabaayun beyond simple difference.
3
Determine permissible predications
Realize that affirmative predication (haml ijab) is never valid between disjoint terms; only negative predication (haml salb) is allowed.
The purpose is to establish the disjointness between the two terms.
4
Analyze distribution in universal negative
In 'No S is P', because S and P are disjoint, both terms are fully distributed (mustaghraq). The author illustrates: 'no human is a horse' implies 'no horse is a human'.
Distribution of the subject forces distribution of the predicate.
5
Analyze distribution in particular negative
In 'Some S is not P', the predicate P is distributed over the negated individuals (e.g., Khalid is not any horse), but the subject S is not fully distributed.
The remaining individuals of S are left unaddressed (maskut 'anhu).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثالثاً : ما بينهما تباين من المفردات التصورية (وهما المختلفان في المعنى وفي الماصدق اختلافاً تاماً، فلا ينطبق أي واحد منهما على أي واحد مما ينطبق عليه الآخر، ويدخل فيهما بهذا الاعتبار المتضادان والمتناقضان) .
والمتباينان لا يحمل أي واحد منهما على الآخر حمل إيجاب مطلقاً، وإنما يحمل على الآخر حمل سلب لإثبات المباينة بينهما.
الأمثلة : فيقال مثلاً :
لا إنسان واحد هو فرس. ولا فرس واحد هو إنسان.
ولا أزلي واحد هو حادث. ولا حادث واحد هو أزلي.
ولا أبيض هو أسود. ولا أسود هو أبيض.
ولا أقل هو مساوٍ. ولا مساوي هو أقل..
وليس عمر هو خالد. وليس خالد هو عمر.
إلى غير ذلك من أمثلة كثيرة.
ويقال أيضاً :
بعض الإنسان ليس بفرس. بعض الفرس ليس بإنسان.
بعض الأبيض ليس بأسود. بعض الأسود ليس بأبيض.
فيكون الحمل من قبيل حمل السلب الجزئي، وهو من الاقتصار الذي قد يفعله المتكلم لغرض يهدف إليه، ويظل كلامه صحيحاً صادقاً، كما ذكرنا في الاقتصار على الحمل الجزئي في جانب الإيجاب.
نظرة ثالثة حول الاستغراق :
ونلاحظ هنا أن استغراق جميع أفراد الموضوع بالسلب قد لزم منه استغراق جميع أفراد المحمول، لأنه إذا لم يكن أي إنسان فرساً، فلن يكن أي فرس إنساناً.
ونلاحظ هنا أيضاً أن سلب المحمول عن بعض أفراد الموضوع قد لزم منه استغراق جميع أفراد المحمول بحكم هذا السلب.
فحين نقول : (بعض الإنسان ليس بفرس) فإن الكلام يعني أن خالداً مثلاً وهو البعض المقصود ليس أي فرد من أفراد الفرس. والباقي من أفراد الموضوع مسكوت عنه، ولا يقتضي السكوت سلباً ولا إيجاباً.
Third: What is between them is disjointness from conceptual singulars (and they are two that differ completely in meaning and in extension, so neither applies to anything that the other applies to, and under this consideration, the contradictory and the contrary are included).
And the two disjoint terms are not predicated of each other by affirmative predication at all; rather, each is predicated of the other by negative predication to establish the disjointness between them.
Examples: It is said, for example:
No human is a horse. No horse is a human.
No eternal is originated. No originated is eternal.
No white is black. No black is white.
No lesser is equal. No equal is lesser.
And Omar is not Khalid. Khalid is not Omar.
And so on with many examples.
And also it is said:
Some human is not a horse. Some horse is not a human.
Some white is not black. Some black is not white.
So the predication is of the type of particular negative predication, and it is a restriction that the speaker may do for a purpose he aims at, and his speech remains correct and true, as we mentioned in the restriction to particular predication in the affirmative side.
A third observation about distribution:
And we note here that the distribution of all individuals of the subject in negation necessitates the distribution of all individuals of the predicate, because if no human is a horse, then no horse is a human.
And we also note here that negating the predicate from some individuals of the subject necessitates the distribution of all individuals of the predicate by virtue of this negation.
For when we say: (Some human is not a horse), the speech means that Khalid, for example, who is the intended part, is not any individual of the individuals of horse. And the rest of the individuals of the subject are passed over in silence, and silence does not necessitate negation nor affirmation.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage defines the third type of logical relation between two terms: 'تباين' (disjoint). Two terms are disjoint if they have no overlap in meaning or extension; they are completely different. The author notes that contraries and contradictories fall under this category. The key rule is that affirmative predication between two disjoint terms is never valid; only negative predication is possible. The author gives examples: 'No human is a horse,' 'No eternal is originated,' etc. Both universal negative and particular negative are valid. Particular negatives are allowed as a restriction (اقتصار) when the speaker only intends to negate some, but the author notes that in universal negative, the distribution of the subject forces distribution of the predicate (e.g., 'no human is a horse' implies 'no horse is a human'). In particular negative ('some human is not a horse'), the negation applies to a specific individual (e.g., Khalid), and the rest are not addressed. The observation about distribution (نظرة ثالثة حول الاستغراق) explains that in a universal negative, both terms are fully distributed. In a particular negative, the predicate is still fully distributed over the individuals being negated, but the subject is not fully distributed.

Key Terms
تباين (tabaayun): A relation where two terms have no common individuals in extension and no common semantic overlap; they are completely different. [Note: Standard across logical texts.]
استغراق (istighraq): Distribution. In this passage, it is noted that in universal negative propositions with disjoint terms, both subject and predicate are distributed. In particular negative, the predicate is distributed over the negated individuals. [Note: Consistent usage.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If 'human' and 'horse' are disjoint, which proposition is true?
A) Every human is a horse
B) Some human is a horse
C) No human is a horse
D) Every horse is a human

Key Takeaway
Disjoint terms only allow negative predication, and in a universal negative both terms are distributed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the following pairs are disjoint: 'iman' and 'kufr', 'salat' and 'sawm', 'tawheed' and 'shirk'. For each, state whether affirmative predication is possible and why.
?
Given the universal negative proposition 'No eternal is originated', verify the distribution of both terms. Write the implied converse and check if it is valid.

Negative Predication of Divergent Terms
Explains predication rules for divergent terms

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify two terms as divergent and determine that only negative predication is valid for them, using both universal and particular forms.

Core Idea
Divergent terms can only be linked by negation—positive predication is impossible.

In logic, terms that have no overlap in their extension are called 'divergent' (mutabayinan). The author lays down a clear rule: you cannot predicate one of the other positively, only negatively. This lesson unpacks that rule with examples from everyday and theological concepts, showing how universal and particular negatives apply.

Guided Path
1
Define divergent terms
Identify two terms that share no common individual in their extension. For example, 'human' and 'horse'—no individual belongs to both categories.
In Arabic: متباينان.
2
State the core rule
Recognize that positive predication (حمل إيجاب) is forbidden between divergent terms. Only negative predication (حمل سلب) can be used to express their relation.
The author says: لا يحمل أي واحد منهما على الآخر حمل إيجاب مطلقاً.
3
Examine universal negative examples
Look at examples like 'No human is a horse.' This is a universal negative proposition, affirming total separation.
Also: لا أزلي واحد هو حادث.
4
Consider particular negative as a restriction
The author notes that one can say 'Some human is not a horse' as a valid particular negative. This is a restriction (iqtisar) for a specific purpose, but still true.
The speaker may focus on a subset for emphasis.

والمتباينان لا يحمل أي واحد منهما على الآخر حمل إيجاب مطلقاً، وإنما يحمل على الآخر حمل سلب لإثبات المباينة بينهما. الأمثلة : فيقال مثلاً : لا إنسان واحد هو فرس. ولا فرس واحد هو إنسان. ولا أزلي واحد هو حادث. ولا حادث واحد هو أزلي. ولا أبيض هو أسود. ولا أسود هو أبيض. ولا أقل هو مساوٍ. ولا مساوي هو أقل.. وليس عمر هو خالد. وليس خالد هو عمر. إلى غير ذلك من أمثلة كثيرة. ويقال أيضاً : بعض الإنسان ليس بفرس. بعض الفرس ليس بإنسان. بعض الأبيض ليس بأسود. بعض الأسود ليس بأبيض. فيكون الحمل من قبيل حمل السلب الجزئي، وهو من الاقتصار الذي قد يفعله المتكلم لغرض يهدف إليه، ويظل كلامه صحيحاً صادقاً، كما ذكرنا في الاقتصار على الحمل الجزئي في جانب الإيجاب. نظرة ثالثة حول الاستغراق : ونلاحظ هنا أن استغراق جميع أفراد الموضوع بالسلب قد لزم منه استغراق جميع أفراد المحمول، لأنه إذا لم يكن أي إنسان فرساً، فلن يكن أي فرس إنساناً. ونلاحظ هنا أيضاً أن سلب المحمول عن بعض أفراد الموضوع قد لزم منه استغراق جميع أفراد المحمول بحكم هذا السلب. فحين نقول : (بعض الإنسان ليس بفرس) فإن الكلام يعني أن خالداً مثلاً وهو البعض المقصود ليس أي فرد من أفراد الفرس. والباقي من أفراد الموضوع مسكوت عنه، ولا يقتضي السكوت سلباً ولا إيجاباً.
The two divergent terms: neither one is predicated of the other by positive predication absolutely, but only by negative predication to prove the divergence between them. Examples: It is said, for instance: No human is a horse. No horse is a human. No eternal is temporal. No temporal is eternal. No white is black. No black is white. No lesser is equal. No equal is lesser. And not: Umar is Khalid. Not Khalid is Umar. And so on from many examples. It is also said: Some human is not a horse. Some horse is not a human. Some white is not black. Some black is not white. So the predication is of the type of particular negative predication, and it is a restriction that the speaker may perform for a purpose he intends, and his statement remains true and correct, as we mentioned regarding restriction to particular predication on the positive side. Third look at distribution: We note here that the distribution of all individuals of the subject by negation necessarily entails the distribution of all individuals of the predicate, because if no human is a horse, then no horse is a human. We also note here that the negation of the predicate from some individuals of the subject necessarily entails the distribution of all individuals of the predicate by virtue of this negation. For when we say: 'Some human is not a horse,' the statement means that Khalid, for example, who is the intended part, is not any individual of horse. And the rest of the individuals of the subject are unmentioned, and silence does not necessitate negation or affirmation.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies the rule for divergent terms (mutabayinan). The author begins by stating the core rule: two divergent terms cannot be positively predicated of each other at all; only negative predication is used to establish their divergence. This is a fundamental principle in logic concerning the relation between terms. The examples given are clear: 'No human is a horse' and similar cases where the two terms have no overlap in extension. The author then introduces the possibility of particular negative predication, where one says 'Some human is not a horse,' which is also valid but is a restriction (iqtisar) for a specific purpose. Following this, the author provides a 'third look' at distribution (istiqraq). Here, he explains two important logical consequences: first, if the universal negative predication distributes all individuals of the subject, it also distributes all individuals of the predicate (as seen in the conversion of 'No human is a horse' to 'No horse is a human'). Second, even in particular negative predication, the negation of the predicate from some individuals of the subject necessarily distributes all individuals of the predicate because the statement implies that the particular individual (e.g., Khalid) is not any member of the predicate. The rest of the subject individuals are left unstated, and silence does not imply either affirmation or negation. This passage is essential for understanding how negation interacts with distribution in categorical propositions.

Key Terms
متباينان
حمل إيجاب
حمل سلب
استغراق

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is a valid predication for two divergent terms?
A) Some human is a horse.
B) No human is a horse.
C) Every human is not a horse (universal negative).
D) Some human is not a horse.

Key Takeaway
Divergent terms can only be connected by negative predication, never positive.

Distribution in Negative Propositions with Divergent Terms
Explains distribution implications of negative predication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the distribution of subject and predicate in universal and particular negative propositions involving divergent terms, and explain the entailment of predicate distribution.

Core Idea
In universal negatives, both terms are distributed; in particular negatives, only the predicate is distributed.

The passage not only gives the rule of predication but also examines the distribution (istiġrāq) of terms in negative propositions. Understanding distribution is crucial for valid reasoning, especially when converting propositions or determining logical consequences. This lesson focuses on the 'third look' at distribution provided by the author.

Guided Path
1
Recall the rule for divergent terms
Remember that divergent terms require negative predication. For example, 'No human is a horse' is a universal negative.
Universal negative: سلب كلي.
2
Analyze distribution in universal negative
In a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No S is P'), both the subject and predicate are distributed. This means the statement applies to every individual of S and every individual of P.
The author says: استغراق جميع أفراد الموضوع بالسلب قد لزم منه استغراق جميع أفراد المحمول.
3
Observe the conversion entailment
From 'No human is a horse' we can validly infer 'No horse is a human' because the predicate was already distributed. This illustrates the logical equivalence of universal negatives.
This is an immediate inference (عكس مستوي).
4
Analyze distribution in particular negative
In a particular negative proposition (e.g., 'Some S is not P'), the subject is not distributed (only some S), but the predicate is distributed. For example, 'Some human is not a horse' means that a particular human (e.g., Khalid) is excluded from every horse.
The author states: سلب المحمول عن بعض أفراد الموضوع قد لزم منه استغراق جميع أفراد المحمول.
5
Explain why predicate is distributed in particular negative
When we say 'Some human is not a horse', we mean that a specific human (Khalid) is not any horse at all. So the negation applies to all individuals of the predicate. The rest of the human individuals are left unstated.
Silence about the rest does not imply affirmation or negation.

Predication Rules for Coextensive Terms
Author defines and exemplifies predication rules for coextensive terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the predication rules for coextensive terms to evaluate the validity of propositions in Islamic logical contexts.

Core Idea
Coextensive terms can only be predicated affirmatively; negating them denies identity.

In logic, some terms have different meanings yet refer to exactly the same individuals. For example, 'human' and 'potentially laughing' are conceptually distinct but extensionally identical. How should we predicate such terms? This passage from the 'Dawabit al-Ma'rifa' lays down the precise rules: only affirmative predication, universal or particular, is allowed. This principle is crucial for correct reasoning in Islamic sciences, especially when dealing with synonymous or co-referential terms in definitions and theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Identify coextensive terms
Find two terms in the passage that differ in meaning (mafhum) but share the same extension (mā ṣadaq). The author gives examples: 'human' and 'potentially laughing', 'Muslim' and 'believer' in God's sight.
Extension refers to all individuals that fall under a term.
2
Check universal affirmative predication
The rule states that coextensive terms can be predicated of each other universally in the affirmative. Write out the A-proposition (Every S is P) and verify that it is true because every member of S is a member of P.
Example: 'Every human is potentially laughing' is true.
3
Check particular affirmative predication
The rule allows restricting to particular affirmative (Some S is P) if needed. Confirm that this is also true, since at least one individual of S is in P.
Example: 'Some Muslim is a believer' is true.
4
Reject negative predications
Negative predications (universal or particular) are forbidden. Why? Because negating a coextensive term would be like saying 'I am not myself' – a contradiction in terms. Test this by attempting to form an E or O proposition and see that it would be false.
Example: 'Every human is not potentially laughing' is false because it denies the identity of extension.
5
Apply to Islamic examples
Use the rule to evaluate other coextensive pairs in Islamic discourse, such as 'alim (knower) and 'arif (gnostic) in Sufi terminology, or 'mustahiq' and 'mustawjib' in fiqh inheritance.
This exercise will sharpen your ability to spot invalid negative predications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رابعاً: ما بينهما تساوٍ من المفردات التصورية (وهما المختلفان في المعنى المتحدان في الماصدق) يحمل كل واحد منهما على الآخر حمل إيجاب كليّ، وقد يُقتصر على الحمل الجزئي إذا تعلق به غرض للمتكلم، ولا يحمل أيّ منهما على الآخر حمل سلب لا كلي ولا جزئي. الأمثلة: فيقال مثلاً: في الحمل الكلي الموجب: كل إنسان ضاحك بالقوة. كل ضاحك بالقوة إنسان. كل مسلم عند الله هو مؤمن. كل مؤمن عند الله هو مسلم. في الحمل الجزئي الموجب: بعض الإنسان ضاحك بالقوة. بعض من هو ضاحك بالقوة إنسان. بعض المسلمين مؤمنون. بعض المؤمنين مسلمون. ولا يقال: في الحمل الكلي السالب: كل إنسان ليس بضاحك بالقوة. كل ضاحك بالقوة ليس بإنسان. أو في الحمل الجزئي السالب: بعض الإنسان ليس بضاحك بالقوة. بعض من هو ضاحك بالقوة ليس بإنسان. وهكذا، فقضايا من هذا النوع كاذبة، نظراً إلى أنها قد سلبت الاتفاق في الماصدق عن متساويين في الماصدق، إذ أفراد المحمول هي أفراد الموضوع نفسها، والسلب أدى إلى سلب الشيء عن نفسه، فهو نظير ما لو قال قائل: أنا لست نفسي.
Fourth: What is coextensive among conceptual singular terms (two terms different in meaning but united in extension): each one of them is predicated of the other by universal positive predication, and may be restricted to particular predication if the speaker has a purpose for that, and neither is predicated of the other by negative predication, whether universal or particular. Examples: It is said, for instance, in universal positive predication: Every human is potentially laughing. Every potentially laughing is human. Every Muslim in God's sight is a believer. Every believer in God's sight is a Muslim. In particular positive predication: Some human is potentially laughing. Some of what is potentially laughing is human. Some Muslims are believers. Some believers are Muslims. And it is not said, in universal negative predication: Every human is not potentially laughing. Every potentially laughing is not human. Or in particular negative predication: Some human is not potentially laughing. Some of what is potentially laughing is not human. And so on, for propositions of this type are false, given that they have negated the agreement in extension regarding two coextensive terms, since the individuals of the predicate are the same individuals of the subject, and the negation led to the negation of a thing from itself; it is similar to if someone said: I am not myself.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage defines the logical rule for coextensive terms (mutasawiyan), which are two terms that differ in meaning (concept) but are identical in extension (apply to exactly the same individuals). The author, in the fourth section, explains that such terms can be predicated of each other universally in the affirmative, and optionally particularly if the speaker has a specific aim. However, they cannot be predicated negatively at all, whether universal or particular, because negating a coextensive term from the other would be negating a thing from itself, which is false. The examples illustrate: 'Every human is potentially laughing' and its converse are true universal affirmatives; 'Some Muslim is a believer' is a true particular affirmative. The author then shows that all negative forms, such as 'Every human is not potentially laughing,' are false because they deny the identity of extension. This is a key principle in immediate inference, particularly for understanding conversion (aks) of propositions: coextensive terms can be simply converted as universal affirmatives.

Key Terms
تساوٍ (tasāwin) – coextension
ما صدق (mā ṣadaq) – extension
حمل كلي (ḥaml kullī) – universal predication
حمل جزئي (ḥaml juz'ī) – particular predication
حمل إيجاب (ḥaml ījāb) – affirmative predication
حمل سلب (ḥaml salb) – negative predication

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Every human is not a potentially laughing thing.
B) Some believer is a Muslim.
C) Some human is not a potentially laughing thing.
D) Every potentially laughing thing is not a human.

Key Takeaway
Coextensive terms can only be predicated affirmatively because negative predication would deny the identity of their extensions, resulting in self-contradiction.

Synonymous Terms (Mutaradifan) in Predication
Author defines and exemplifies predication rules for synonymous terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether two terms are synonymous (mutaradifan) and apply the rule of mutual positive predication.

Core Idea
Synonymous terms differ in wording but share meaning; they always predicate positively, never negatively.

This lesson covers the fifth type of word relation: synonymy. In Islamic logic, terms that are different in wording but identical in meaning have a simple rule of mutual predication. Understanding this rule helps avoid confusion when dealing with multiple names for the same concept, such as in legal definitions or Quranic terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Look at the two words given. They must be different in their lexical form (different letters/sound) but refer to the same meaning.
If the two words are identical, they are not synonyms; they are repetitions.
2
Check unity of meaning (maʿnā)
Verify whether the two words denote exactly the same concept, not just overlapping or related meanings. If they share the same maʿnā, they are synonyms (mutarādifān).
Synonyms are a subset of coextensive terms; all synonyms are coextensive but not vice versa.
3
Apply positive predication
If the terms are synonymous, each can be predicated of the other in an affirmative proposition. For example, 'Every asad is a layth' is true if both mean 'lion'.
4
Forbid negative predication
Because of their identity in meaning, a negative predication (e.g., 'No asad is a layth') would be false. Neither term can be negated of the other.
This rule follows from the law of non-contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خامساً: ما بينهما ترادف من الألفاظ (وهما المختلفان في اللفظ المتحدان في المعنى) يحمل كل واحد منهما على الآخر حمل إيجاب، ولا يحمل أي منهما على الآخر حمل سلب، لاتحادهما في المعنى.
Fifth: What is synonymous among utterances (two terms different in wording but united in meaning): each one of them is predicated of the other by positive predication, and neither is predicated of the other by negative predication, due to their unity in meaning.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth and final type of relation between terms: synonymous terms (mutaradifan). The author defines them as two different words (لفظ) that share the same meaning (معنى). For example, 'Lion' and 'Asad' in Arabic are synonyms. The rule is straightforward: since they have identical meaning, they can be predicated of each other positively (e.g., 'Every lion is an asad' is true), but they cannot be predicated negatively at all, because negating one from the other would contradict the sameness of meaning. This is analogous to the rule for coextensive terms in the previous section, but with the additional emphasis on meaning rather than extension. In the context of Islamic logic, this helps distinguish between conceptual identity (meaning) and extensional identity (application). Note that synonymous terms are a subset of coextensive terms (if they share meaning, they necessarily share extension), but the converse is not true.

Key Terms
ترادف
معنى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) 'Al-Kāfir' (disbeliever) and 'Al-Mu’min' (believer)

Key Takeaway
Synonymous terms are different words with identical meaning; they must be predicated of each other positively, never negatively.

Identifying Mutual Similarity (Tamathul) in Predication
Defines the sixth type of predication (tamathul) with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify tamathul (mutual similarity) between two terms and verify their convertibility.

Core Idea
Tamathul means two terms are convertible: each can be predicated of the other affirmatively.

We have seen types of predication based on inclusion, opposition, etc. Now we encounter tamathul, where two terms are coextensive. They are like synonyms in meaning. The key test: can you swap subject and predicate and still have a true affirmative proposition? This lesson teaches you to recognize tamathul and distinguish it from other relations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Find the subject and predicate in the proposition being examined. They are the two terms that are claimed to be similar.
2
Check for coextension
Determine if the two terms lead to the same meaning in the result. That is, are they true of exactly the same things? In the passage, terms that are 'like synonyms and coextensive' are tamathul.
Coextension means every instance of one is an instance of the other and vice versa.
3
Test convertibility
Swap the subject and predicate. If the resulting proposition is true (affirmative), then the terms are convertible, confirming tamathul. The passage shows examples where the inversion holds true.
If the swapped version is false or meaningless, the terms are not tamathul.
4
Verify negation is false
Ensure that a negative predication of one term on the other would be false. For tamathul, negation would deny their similarity, making the proposition false.
This is a crucial test: if 'A is not B' is true, then A and B are not tamathul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سادساً: ما بينهما تماثل، وهما المؤديان إلى معنى واحد في النتيجة، وهما كالمترادفين وكالمتساويين، يحمل كل واحد منهما على الآخر حمل إيجاب لا حمل سلب، لتماثلهما في النتيجة، فيقال مثلاً خمسة مضروبة بخمسة هي خمسة وعشرون، أو هي خمسة مضاف إليها عشرة وعشرة. ويقال: الزوج والفرد هما العدد. ويقال: الوجوب والإمكان والاستحالة هي أحكام العقل المتعلقة بالوجود والعدم. وتعكس هذه الأمثلة فيجعل الموضوع محمولاً والمحمول موضوعاً ويكون الحمل صادقاً. ولا يصح أن يكون شيء منها سالباً لأن القضايا حينئذٍ تكون كاذبة، إذ يؤدي السلب إلى نفي التماثل عن المتماثلين.
Sixthly: (The relation) between them is similarity, and they are those that lead to one meaning in the result, and they are like synonyms and like coextensive terms. Each one of them is predicated upon the other with affirmative predication, not negative predication, due to their similarity in the result. So it is said for example: Five multiplied by five is twenty-five, or it is five added to ten and ten. And it is said: The even and the odd are number. And it is said: Necessity, possibility, and impossibility are the judgments of the intellect pertaining to existence and non-existence. And these examples are inverted such that the subject becomes predicate and the predicate becomes subject, and the predication is true. And it is not correct for any of them to be negative because the propositions would then be false, since the negation leads to denying similarity between similar things.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth type of relation between two conceptual singulars: mutual similarity (tamathul). Here, the two terms are such that they lead to the same meaning in the result, i.e., they are coextensive in reference. The author compares them to synonyms and coextensive terms, meaning that each can be truly predicated of the other in an affirmative statement, never in a negative one. Several examples are given: a mathematical equivalence (5×5 = 25, which is also 5+10+10), a mathematical classification (even and odd are both numbers), and a logical classification (the three modal judgments are all judgments of the intellect about existence). The key property is that the subject and predicate can be swapped while preserving truth; this is the hallmark of coextensive terms. Negation is forbidden because it would deny the very similarity that defines the relation.

مترادفان
متساويان

Key Takeaway
Tamathul is the relation of coextension: two terms that can be predicated of each other affirmatively, and their negation is false.

Rules of Distribution of Subject and Predicate
Author enumerates the four rules of term distribution in categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which terms are distributed in each of the four categorical proposition types (A, E, I, O) and explain the underlying reasoning.

Core Idea
Distribution tells you whether a term covers all its members; it is the key to valid syllogisms.

In this passage, the author lays down the four foundational rules that determine when a term in a categorical proposition refers to all members of its class. Understanding these rules is essential because distribution governs whether immediate inferences (like conversion) are valid and whether a syllogism's middle term is properly connected. Let's unpack each rule carefully.

Guided Path
1
Understand distribution (istighraq)
Distribution means a term refers to every member of its class. The author says a term may be universal distributed, universal undistributed, or particular (always distributed). The quantifier (sūr) determines distribution.
The opposite of distributed is 'not distributed' or 'partial'.
2
Rule for Universal Affirmative (A)
For propositions like 'Every S is P', the subject is distributed (all S), but the predicate is not distributed (unless the predicate itself is universal, like 'All stars are all adornment'). Example: 'Every human is an animal' – 'human' is distributed, 'animal' is not.
If the predicate also has a universal quantifier, then it is distributed.
3
Rule for Universal Negative (E)
For 'No S is P', both subject and predicate are distributed. The author says this follows by entailment (luzum): if any P were S, the proposition would be false. So the predicate must cover all its individuals.
Example: 'No stone is an animal' – both 'stone' and 'animal' are distributed.
4
Rules for Particular propositions (I and O)
Particular affirmative ('Some S is P') distributes neither subject nor predicate. Particular negative ('Some S is not P') does not distribute subject but does distribute predicate. The author explains that for O, entailment shows the predicate must be distributed because if even one individual of the predicate were included in the subject, the negation would be false.
Memorize: I distributes nothing; O distributes predicate only.
5
Practice with Islamic examples
Take a proposition from kalam or fiqh, identify its type (A, E, I, O), and state which terms are distributed. For instance, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (A): subject distributed, predicate not. 'No prayer is valid without purification' (E): both distributed.
This skill is essential for checking syllogistic validity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضوابط استغراق الموضوع والمحمول لأفرادهما :
من دراستنا لضوابط الحمل في القضايا الحملية تبين لنا أن الموضوع قد يكون كلياً مستغرقاً لجميع أفراده، وقد يكون كلياً غير مستغرق، وقد يكون شخصياً، والشخصي مستغرق لا محالة، وأن المحمول كذلك قد يكون كلياً مستغرقاً لجميع أفراده، وقد يكون كلياً غير مستغرق لجميع أفراده، وقد يكون شخصياً، والشخصي مستغرق لا محالة، وحول الاستغراق وعدمه نستخلص الضوابط التالية :
الضابط الأول: الكلية الموجبة تفيد استغراق موضوعها لجميع أفراده، ولا تفيد استغراق محمولها إلا إذا كان المحمول مسوّراً بسور كلي أيضاً.
الأمثلة :
كل إنسان حيوان :
كل نجم مضيء :
كلية موجبة تفيد استغراق موضوعها فقط لجميع أفراده، ولا تفيد استغراق محمولها لجميع أفراده
كل نجوم السماء وكواكبها هي كل زينتها :
كل ما لدينا من مال هو كل ما أنفقنا في الرحلة :
وبقوة هذه شخصية الموضوع والمحمول. مثل : أبو حفص عمر :
كلية موجبة تفيد استغراق موضوعها لجميع أفراده، وتفيد استغراق محمولها لجميع أفراده، لأن المحمول مسوّر أيضاً بسور كلي
الضابط الثاني : الكلية السالبة تفيد استغراق موضوعها لجميع أفراده، ويلزم منه استغراق محمولها لجميع أفراده .
الأمثلة :
لا شيء من الحجر بحيوان :
لا شيء من التفاح بزيتون :
كلية سالبة تفيد استغراق موضوعها ومحمولها لأفرادهما، فالموضوع بمقتضى السور الكلي السالب والمحمول بمقتضى اللزوم، لأنه لو وجد شيء من الزيتون تفاحاً لكذبت القضية، وكذلك لو وجد شيء من الحيوان حجراً
الضابط الثالث : الجزئية الموجبة لا تفيد استغراق موضوعها ولا محمولها لجميع أفرادهما .
الأمثلة :
بعض الطلاب ناجحون :
بعض المعدن نحاس :
فلا استغراق في الموضوع بدليل السور الجزئي، ولا استغراق في المحمول إذ لا دليل عليه ؛ ولا يقتضيه اللزوم
الضابط الرابع : الجزئية السالبة لا تفيد استغراق موضوعها لجميع أفراده، ولكن تفيد استغراق محمولها لجميع أفراده .
الأمثلة :
بعض العلماء ليسوا بعاملين :
بعض العاملين ليسوا بمخلصين :
فلا استغراق في الموضوع لأفراده بدليل السور الجزئي، وأما استغراق المحمول لأفراده فقد دل عليه اللزوم
Rules of distribution of the subject and predicate over their individuals: From our study of the rules of predication in categorical propositions, it became clear to us that the subject may be universal distributed over all its individuals, may be universal not distributed, and may be particular, and the particular is necessarily distributed; and similarly the predicate may be universal distributed over all its individuals, may be universal not distributed over all its individuals, and may be particular, and the particular is necessarily distributed. Regarding distribution and non-distribution, we derive the following rules: first rule: The universal affirmative conveys distribution of its subject over all its individuals, and does not convey distribution of its predicate unless the predicate is also quantified with a universal quantifier. Examples: Every human is an animal; every star is luminous: A universal affirmative conveys distribution of its subject only over all its individuals, and does not convey distribution of its predicate over all its individuals. All the stars of the sky and their planets are all its adornment; all the wealth we have is all we spent on the trip: And by force of these, the subject and predicate are particular. Example: Abu Hafs Umar: A universal affirmative conveys distribution of its subject over all its individuals, and conveys distribution of its predicate over all its individuals, because the predicate is also quantified with a universal quantifier. Second rule: The universal negative conveys distribution of its subject over all its individuals, and from that follows distribution of its predicate over all its individuals. Examples: No stone is an animal; no apple is an olive: A universal negative conveys distribution of its subject and its predicate over their individuals: the subject by virtue of the universal negative quantifier, and the predicate by virtue of entailment, because if there were some olive that is an apple, the proposition would be false, and similarly if there were some animal that is a stone. Third rule: The particular affirmative conveys distribution neither of its subject nor of its predicate over all their individuals. Examples: Some students are successful; some minerals are copper: So there is no distribution in the subject due to the particular quantifier, and no distribution in the predicate since there is no evidence for it, and entailment does not require it. Fourth rule: The particular negative does not convey distribution of its subject over all its individuals, but conveys distribution of its predicate over all its individuals. Examples: Some scholars are not workers; some workers are not sincere: So there is no distribution in the subject over its individuals due to the particular quantifier, but as for distribution of the predicate over its individuals, entailment indicates it.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage defines the four rules of distribution (استغراق) for terms in categorical propositions. Distribution indicates whether a term refers to all members of its class or not. The author begins by clarifying that subject and predicate can each be universal distributed, universal undistributed, or particular (always distributed). Then four rules are enumerated: (1) Universal affirmative (A) distributes its subject but not its predicate, unless the predicate itself is universal (e.g., 'Every human is an animal' distributes 'human' but not 'animal'; but 'All stars are all the adornment' distributes both because the predicate is universal). (2) Universal negative (E) distributes both subject and predicate necessarily—if even one member of the predicate were the subject, the proposition would be false. (3) Particular affirmative (I) distributes neither term. (4) Particular negative (O) does not distribute its subject but distributes its predicate—this follows by entailment: if the predicate were not distributed, the subject could be entirely outside it, but negation requires that no subject is in the predicate, so the predicate must be taken in full. These rules are essential for understanding immediate inferences (conversion, obversion) and for checking validity of syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
The four distribution rules are: Universal affirmative (A) distributes subject only; Universal negative (E) distributes both; Particular affirmative (I) distributes neither; Particular negative (O) distributes predicate only.

Rule for Distribution in Particular Affirmative Propositions
Author states the third rule of quantification for particular affirmatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which terms are distributed in a particular affirmative proposition using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
In a particular affirmative, neither the subject nor the predicate is distributed.

This lesson examines the third rule of quantification: the particular affirmative proposition does not distribute its subject or its predicate. Understanding this rule is essential for evaluating the validity of syllogisms in Islamic logic, especially when premises involve particular claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Check the quantifier: if it says 'some' (ba'd) or 'one of' (wāḥid), the proposition is particular. Also check the quality: affirmative (positive) or negative.
For this rule, we focus on particular affirmative (e.g., 'Some scholars are knowledgeable').
2
Examine the subject distribution
The subject is not distributed because the quantifier 'some' indicates only a part of the subject class is being referred to. In 'Some scholars are knowledgeable', not every scholar is mentioned.
The particular quantifier (al-sawr al-juz'ī) prevents distribution of the subject.
3
Examine the predicate distribution
The predicate is also not distributed because there is no evidence that it applies to all its individuals. In 'Some scholars are knowledgeable', 'knowledgeable' does not include all knowledgeable people; it is not necessary that every knowledgeable person is a scholar.
The author says 'no evidence' and 'necessity (al-luzum) does not require it'.
4
Apply the rule to an Islamic example
Take 'Some believers are sincere'. The subject 'believers' is not all believers; the predicate 'sincere' is not all sincere people. Neither term is distributed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضابط الثالث : الجزئية الموجبة لا تفيد استغراق موضوعها ولا محمولها لجميع أفرادهما . الأمثلة : بعض الطلاب ناجحون : بعض المعدن نحاس : فلا استغراق في الموضوع بدليل السور الجزئي، ولا استغراق في المحمول إذ لا دليل عليه ؛ ولا يقتضيه اللزوم الضابط الرابع : الجزئية السالبة لا تفيد استغراق موضوعها لجميع أفراده، ولكن تفيد استغراق محمولها لجميع أفراده . الأمثلة : بعض العلماء ليسوا بعاملين : بعض العاملين ليسوا بمخلصين : فلا استغراق في الموضوع لأفراده بدليل السور الجزئي، وأما استغراق المحمول لأفراده فقد دل عليه اللزوم
The third rule: The particular affirmative does not imply the distribution of its subject nor its predicate for all their individuals. Examples: Some students are successful. Some mineral is copper. So there is no distribution of the subject due to the particular quantifier, and no distribution of the predicate since there is no evidence for it; and necessity does not require it. The fourth rule: The particular negative does not imply the distribution of its subject for all its individuals, but it does imply the distribution of its predicate for all its individuals. Examples: Some scholars are not workers. Some workers are not sincere. So there is no distribution of the subject for its individuals due to the particular quantifier, and as for the distribution of the predicate for its individuals, necessity has indicated it.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage defines the third and fourth rules of quantification regarding the distribution (istiqhra’) of terms in particular propositions. The author first states the third rule: a particular affirmative proposition does not distribute its subject nor its predicate. The examples given, 'Some students are successful' and 'Some mineral is copper', illustrate that the subject is not taken universally because of the particular quantifier 'some'. The predicate also is not distributed because there is no logical necessity requiring that every successful person is a student, or that every copper piece is mineral. The author then presents the fourth rule: a particular negative proposition does not distribute its subject, but it does distribute its predicate. The examples 'Some scholars are not workers' and 'Some workers are not sincere' show that the subject is still particular, but the predicate is taken universally: the negation applies to all individuals of the predicate. In 'Some scholars are not workers', the predicate 'workers' is distributed because the scholars who are not workers are excluded from the entire class of workers. The author concludes that necessity (al-luzum) indicates the distribution of the predicate. These rules are fundamental for understanding conversion and syllogistic validity.

Rule for Distribution in Particular Negative Propositions
Author states the fourth rule of quantification for particular negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which terms are distributed in a particular negative proposition using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
In a particular negative, the subject is not distributed but the predicate is distributed.

This lesson covers the fourth rule of quantification: the particular negative proposition does not distribute its subject but does distribute its predicate. Mastering this rule is vital for correctly analyzing syllogisms in Islamic logic, especially when dealing with negative statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Check the quantifier: 'some' or 'a' indicates particularity. Check the quality: negative (e.g., 'is not', 'are not'). This is a particular negative proposition.
For this rule, we focus on 'Some A is not B'.
2
Examine the subject distribution
The subject is not distributed because the quantifier 'some' indicates only a part of the subject class. In 'Some scholars are not workers', not all scholars are referred to; only a subset.
The particular quantifier prevents distribution of the subject.
3
Examine the predicate distribution
The predicate is distributed because the negation applies to the entire class of the predicate. In 'Some scholars are not workers', the predicate 'workers' is taken universally: the scholars excluded are not part of the whole set of workers. Necessity (al-luzum) indicates this distribution.
The author says 'necessity has indicated it' meaning it follows logically.
4
Apply the rule to an Islamic example
Take 'Some Muslims are not practicing'. The subject 'Muslims' is not distributed (not all Muslims), but the predicate 'practicing' is distributed (the negation covers all practicing individuals).

Implication in Conditionals as Immediate Perception
Author introduces implication in conditionals as immediate perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between immediate perception of implication in conditional propositions and mediated syllogistic reasoning, using examples from Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Implication in conditionals is perceived directly, not derived through syllogism.

In this passage, the author explains that when we judge a conditional statement like 'If Z, then W,' we do not always need a syllogism to recognize the connection. Instead, the implication (luzum) is often grasped immediately—a direct mental perception. This lesson unpacks what that means and how it differs from the indirect reasoning of syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim
The passage begins by stating that 'implication in conditional propositions is a type of immediate perception.' Underline this claim. The author is asserting that the mental operation of perceiving the link between two propositions in a conditional is direct, not mediated.
2
Understand the process of perceiving implication
The author describes how the observer notices that one proposition is inseparably linked to another—whether in existence, non-existence, or both. List the types of linkage mentioned: inseparable in existence and non-existence, existence of one implies non-existence of the other, non-existence implies existence, and contradictory linkage (one exists iff the other does not).
3
Distinguish from syllogism
The author explicitly contrasts this with syllogism: syllogism produces a new proposition by observing inclusion of a ruling through a middle term; it is indirect. Implication, however, is immediate—no middle term is needed. Highlight the sentence: 'The syllogism is indirect reasoning, whereas the judgment of implication or its absence in propositions is via immediate perception.'
4
Apply the distinction
Now consider an example: in the statement 'If the world is temporally originated, then it has a Creator.' Is the implication here perceived immediately, or does it require a syllogism? According to the author, if you see the connection directly (as a necessary link), it is immediate perception. If you need a middle term (e.g., 'everything originated needs a creator'), then it becomes syllogistic.

(۱۲) اللزوم في القضايا الشرطية من أنواع الإدراك المباشر عملية عقلية يدرك بها الناظر اللزوم في القضايا. إن الباحث الناظر في الواقع المدرك بالحس، أو المدرك بالنظر العقلي المجرد، ليلاحظ أن قضية مثلاً مقترنة بأخرى اقتراناً غير منفك، في الوجود، أو في العدم، أو في كليهما، أو مقترن وجود إحداهما بانعدام الأخرى، أو مقترن انعدام إحداهما بوجود الأخرى، أو اقترانهما اقتران تناقض فأيهما وجد انعدم الآخر وأيهما انعدم وجد الآخر. فيربط بين القضيتين برباط شرطي يدلّ به على ما أدركه عن الواقع لدى نظره الفكري في القضيتين، فالقضيتان معاً قد كانتا مادة الإدراك المباشر، وعندئذٍ يصدر حكماً شرطياً بما أدركه من اقتران، ويعقد بين القضيتين، ويجعل منهما قضية شرطية واحدة. ولا تشبه هذه العملية العقلية ما يجري في القياس، لأن القياس يقدم قضية جديدة عن طريق ملاحظة شمول حكم قضية لقضية أخرى لا يوجد فيها هذا الحكم، فالقياس استدلال غير مباشر، أما الحكم باللزوم أو عدمه في القضايا فطريقه الإدراك المباشر، ما لم يستخدم لذلك القياس أو دليل آخر من أدلة الاستدلال. إذن فمن الإدراك المباشر هذه العملية العقلية التي يدرك بها الناظر
(12) Implication in conditional propositions is a type of immediate perception, a mental operation by which the observer perceives implication in propositions. Indeed, the researcher who observes reality perceived by the senses, or perceived by abstract intellectual reflection, notices that a proposition, for example, is conjoined with another in an inseparable conjunction, in existence, or in non-existence, or in both; or the existence of one is conjoined with the non-existence of the other; or the non-existence of one is conjoined with the existence of the other; or their conjunction is a conjunction of contradiction such that whichever exists, the other is non-existent, and whichever is non-existent, the other exists. So he connects the two propositions with a conditional connector indicating what he perceived about reality through his intellectual reflection on the two propositions. The two propositions together constitute the material of immediate perception. At that point he issues a conditional judgment based on what he perceived of the conjunction, and he links the two propositions, making them into one conditional proposition. This mental operation does not resemble what occurs in syllogism, because the syllogism produces a new proposition by observing the inclusion of the ruling of one proposition in another proposition that does not contain this ruling. So the syllogism is indirect reasoning, whereas the judgment of implication or its absence in propositions is via immediate perception, unless syllogism or another proof from the proofs of reasoning is used for that. Therefore, this mental operation by which the observer perceives is from immediate perception.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage introduces the concept of implication (luzum) in conditional propositions as a form of immediate perception (idrak mubashir). The author begins by stating that implication in conditionals is a type of immediate perception—a mental operation by which the observer perceives the implication between propositions. The author then describes the process: the researcher, whether observing sensible reality or engaging in abstract intellectual reflection, notices that one proposition is inseparably linked to another. This linkage can be: inseparable in existence and non-existence (e.g., If A then B, and conversely), or linked such that the existence of one implies the non-existence of the other, or the non-existence of one implies the existence of the other, or they are contradictory such that only one can be true. The researcher then connects the two propositions with a conditional connector (ribat sharti) and forms a single conditional proposition. The author emphasizes that this process is distinct from syllogism (qiyas). In syllogism, we derive a new proposition indirectly through the inclusion of a ruling from one proposition in another. In contrast, the judgment of implication or its absence is immediate—it is perceived directly, without needing a middle term. The author concludes that this mental operation is a type of immediate perception. This passage lays the epistemological foundation for conditional propositions, grounding their validity in direct intellectual intuition.
