Conditional Propositions and the Three Types of Implication
Author defines conditional propositions and classifies three types of implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of implication (luzum ittifaqi, sababi, daruri 'aqli) and identify them in examples from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
Conditional propositions are built on real connections—accidental, causal, or rational—not mere logical form.

This passage defines conditional propositions and reveals the mental process of forming them from real-world observation. It then classifies the types of implication that ground these conditionals. Understanding these types is essential for evaluating the strength of arguments in kalam and fiqh, where the basis of a conditional connection determines its validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the components of a conditional proposition
A conditional proposition consists of two atomic propositions joined by a conditional bond (irtibat sharti). Find the two parts in each example given by the author.
The bond can be conjunctive (if-then) or disjunctive (either-or).
2
Analyze the mental process in the day/sun example
Trace how the mind observes real-world concomitance (talazum) between daytime and sunrise, then expresses this as a conditional. Note that this is empirical, not purely logical.
The author emphasizes that we perceive the connection directly before formulating the conditional.
3
Distinguish the three types of implication
The author lists three motives (dawa'i) of implication: accidental (ittifaqi), causal (sababi), and rational necessary (daruri 'aqli). For each, note the basis: no rational necessity, cause-effect, or pure rational necessity.
Accidental implications are one-sided (e.g., raven's feather), while causal and rational implications may be reciprocal.
4
Classify a new example
Given a conditional proposition from an Islamic text, determine which type of implication it uses based on the criteria in the passage.
For instance, 'If a person is in a state of janaba, he may not pray' is based on divine command (causal via shari'a).

الباحث واقع اقتران بين قضيتين، فيصدر بالاستناد إليها حكماً شرطياً، فيقول مثلاً : أ ـ إذا كان النهار موجوداً فالشمس طالعة . ب ـ لو تعدّدت الآلهة تعدّداً بحق لفسد نظام الكون . جـ ـ إما أن يكون العدد زوجاً أو فرداً . د ـ الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوماً . هـ ـ الكلمة إما اسم أو فعل أو حرف . و ـ كلما كان اللون أبيض فهو لا أسود . إلى غير ذلك من أمثلة شرطية كثيرة . فلنحلل العملية العقلية في المثال الأول : كلنا ندرك وجود النهار وامتداد بياضه وسلامة الرؤية فيه في أغلب الأحيان، وندرك أن وجوده مقارن لطلوع الشمس وامتداد أشعتها باتجاه مكان النهار في الأرض، ثم ندرك ذهاب النهار وقدوم الليل وإقبال سواده وظلمته، وندرك أن ذهاب النهار مقارن لغروب الشمس وانحجاب أشعتها عن الأرض . بعد هذه الإدراكات نعقد مباشرة بين قضية وجود النهار وطلوع الشمس ارتباطاً تلازمياً، وبين قضية وجود الليل وغروب الشمس ارتباطاً تلازمياً . ونعلق كل قضية بينها وبين صاحبتها تلازم برباط شرطي، فنقول : إذا كانت الشمس طالعة في الأفق فالنهار موجود . إذا كان النهار موجوداً فالشمس طالعة في الأفق . إذا كانت الشمس غاربة فالنهار غير موجود . إذا كانت الشمس غاربة فالليل موجود . إذا كان الليل موجوداً فالشمس غاربة . إذا كانت الشمس طالعة في الأفق فالليل غير موجود . كل هذه القضايا أدركناها بشكل مباشر من إدراك التلازم في الواقع بين طلوع الشمس والنهار، وغروبها والليل . والعملية التي قمنا بها كانت عملية عقلية صرفاً. ولدى تحليل العملية العقلية في المثال الثالث نلاحظ ما يلي: نحصي الأعداد الصحيحة فنرى أن بعضها فرد وبعضها زوج فنعقد مباشرة ارتباطاً شرطياً عادياً بين الزوج والفرد، فنقول: العدد الصحيح إما زوج وإما فرد. دواعي اللزوم: واللزوم إما أن يكون اتفاقياً في الواقع ليس له مقتض عقلي، وإما أن يكون له مقتض عقلي من واقع نظام الأسباب والمسببات؛ أو من الضرورة العقلية البحتة. ١ - فالاتفاقي كلزوم السواد لريش الغراب، فيلزم من كون الطائر غراباً أن يكون ريشه أسود اللون، واللزوم هنا من طرف واحد، لأنه لا يلزم أنه كلما وجد لون أسود أن يكون هو لون ريش غراب. ٢ - وما له مقتض عقلي من واقع نظام الأسباب والمسببات، مثاله: إذا انخفضت درجة الحرارة إلى ما تحت الصفر تجمد الماء، فاللزوم الملاحظ في هذا المثال لزوم سببي، لأن انخفاض درجة الحرارة كان سبباً في تجمد الماء، فلزم من الانخفاض التجمد، وهذا التجمد له مقتض عقلي من واقع نظام الأسباب والمسببات. ٣ - وما له مقتض عقلي من الضرورة العقلية، مثاله: • إذا كان الشيء موجوداً فهو ليس معدوماً قطعاً، وإذا كان معدوماً فهو غير موجود قطعاً. • العدد الصحيح إما زوج وإما فرد. • العددان إما متساويان أو أحدهما أكثر والثاني أقل. • الجسمان إما متساويان أو أحدهما أكبر والثاني أصغر. فاللزوم الملاحظ في هذه الأمثلة لزوم اقتضته الضرورة العقلية.
The researcher [observes] an actual conjunction between two propositions, and then based on them issues a conditional judgment, saying for example: A) If daytime exists, then the sun is rising. B) If there were truly multiple gods, the order of the universe would be corrupted. C) The number is either even or odd. D) A thing is either existent or non-existent. E) A word is either noun, verb, or particle. F) Whenever a color is white, it is not black. And many other conditional examples. Let us analyze the mental process in the first example: We all perceive the existence of daytime, the extension of its brightness, and the clarity of vision in it most of the time, and we perceive that its existence is concomitant with the rising of the sun and the extension of its rays toward the place of daytime on Earth. Then we perceive the departure of daytime and the arrival of night, the coming of its blackness and darkness, and we perceive that the departure of daytime is concomitant with the setting of the sun and the veiling of its rays from the Earth. After these perceptions, we directly establish between the proposition of the existence of daytime and the rising of the sun a concomitant connection, and between the proposition of the existence of night and the setting of the sun a concomitant connection. And we attach each proposition with its companion by a conditional bond, saying: If the sun is rising on the horizon, then daytime exists. If daytime exists, then the sun is rising on the horizon. If the sun is setting, then daytime does not exist. If the sun is setting, then night exists. If night exists, then the sun is setting. If the sun is rising on the horizon, then night does not exist. All these propositions we perceived directly from perceiving the concomitance in reality between the rising of the sun and daytime, and its setting and night. And the process we performed was a purely mental process. Upon analyzing the mental process in the third example, we observe the following: We count the integers and see that some are odd and some are even, so we directly establish an ordinary conditional connection between even and odd, saying: An integer is either even or odd. Motives of implication: Implication is either accidental in reality, having no rational basis, or it has a rational basis from the reality of the system of causes and effects, or from pure rational necessity. 1) Accidental, like the implication of blackness for the raven's feather: from the bird being a raven, it follows that its feather is black, and the implication here is one-sided, because it does not follow that whenever black is found, it is the color of a raven's feather. 2) What has a rational basis from the reality of the system of causes and effects: for example, if the temperature drops below zero, water freezes. The implication observed in this example is a causal implication, because the drop in temperature was a cause for the freezing of water, so freezing followed from the drop, and this freezing has a rational basis from the reality of the system of causes and effects. 3) What has a rational basis from pure rational necessity: for example, if a thing exists, it is definitely not non-existent, and if it is non-existent, it is definitely not existent. An integer is either even or odd. Two numbers are either equal or one is greater and the other lesser. Two bodies are either equal or one is larger and the other smaller. So the implication observed in these examples is an implication necessitated by rational necessity.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage defines conditional propositions (qadaya shartiyya) and classifies the types of implication (luzum) that underlie them. The author begins by giving six examples of conditional propositions: two conditional (if-then), two disjunctive (either-or), one enumerative (word types), and one negative conditional (whenever not). These illustrate the range of conditionals. Then the author analyzes the mental process for the first example (day and sun), showing how we perceive a real-world concomitance (talazum) between the existence of daytime and the rising of the sun, and then we express this as a conditional bond. Similarly, for the third example (even/odd), we inductively observe that all integers are either even or odd, and we formulate a disjunctive conditional. Next, the author discusses the motives (dawa'i) of implication, i.e., the basis of the connection. Three types are enumerated: (1) Accidental (ittifaqi): no rational necessity, e.g., blackness on raven's feather—it is one-sided, not reciprocal. (2) Causal (sababi): based on a cause-effect system, e.g., freezing water due to temperature drop. (3) Rational necessary (darura 'aqliyya): pure logical necessity, e.g., existence implies non-existence of non-existence. The author emphasizes that the examples given are implications necessitated by reason. This classification is crucial in logic because it distinguishes between merely empirical connections and necessary logical laws. Students should note that in Islamic logic, conditional propositions are not merely material conditionals; they require a real connection. For example, in fiqh, 'If a person is in a state of major impurity (janaba), he may not pray' is based on a divine cause (hukm shar'i), which falls under the causal type. Thus, understanding the type of implication helps evaluate the strength of arguments using conditionals.

Three Types of Logical Necessity
Classifies logical necessity into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given logical necessity as coincidental, causal, or purely rational using the criteria from this passage.

Core Idea
Logical necessity arises from coincidence, causality, or pure reason — only the last is truly universal.

This passage from Dawabit al-Ma'rifa categorises logical necessity (luzum) into three kinds. Understanding these categories is crucial for evaluating arguments: if you mistake a coincidental connection for a causal one, your reasoning may be flawed. This lesson will help you recognise each type through its defining characteristics and examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two items connected
Find the two states or propositions between which necessity is claimed. For example, 'being a crow' and 'having black feathers'.
2
Check the direction of necessity
Is the necessity one-sided or two-sided? Coincidental necessity is typically one-sided: crow implies black feathers, but black feathers do not imply crow. Causal and rational necessities are often two-sided.
3
Determine the basis: observation or reason
Ask: does this connection rely on empirical observation of causes and effects (like water freezing when cold), or is it self-evident to reason (like a number being even or odd)? If it depends on observed regularity, it is causal. If it is known by pure reason without empirical input, it is rational.
4
If neither, classify as coincidental
If the connection is merely observed in reality but has no rational or causal basis (like blackness of crow’s feathers), it is coincidental. Such necessities are not universal laws.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دواعي اللزوم:
واللزوم إما أن يكون اتفاقياً في الواقع ليس له مقتض عقلي، وإما أن يكون له مقتض عقلي من واقع نظام الأسباب والمسببات؛ أو من الضرورة العقلية البحتة.
١ - فالاتفاقي كلزوم السواد لريش الغراب، فيلزم من كون الطائر غراباً أن يكون ريشه أسود اللون، واللزوم هنا من طرف واحد، لأنه لا يلزم أنه كلما وجد لون أسود أن يكون هو لون ريش غراب.
٢ - وما له مقتض عقلي من واقع نظام الأسباب والمسببات، مثاله: إذا انخفضت درجة الحرارة إلى ما تحت الصفر تجمد الماء، فاللزوم الملاحظ في هذا المثال لزوم سببي، لأن انخفاض درجة الحرارة كان سبباً في تجمد الماء، فلزم من الانخفاض التجمد، وهذا التجمد له مقتض عقلي من واقع نظام الأسباب والمسببات.
٣ - وما له مقتض عقلي من الضرورة العقلية، مثاله:
• إذا كان الشيء موجوداً فهو ليس معدوماً قطعاً، وإذا كان معدوماً فهو غير موجود قطعاً.
• العدد الصحيح إما زوج وإما فرد.
• العددان إما متساويان أو أحدهما أكثر والثاني أقل.
• الجسمان إما متساويان أو أحدهما أكبر والثاني أصغر.
فاللزوم الملاحظ في هذه الأمثلة لزوم اقتضته الضرورة العقلية.
Motivations of necessity:
And necessity is either coincidental in reality having no rational basis, or it has a rational basis from the reality of the system of causes and effects; or from pure rational necessity.
1 - The coincidental, like the necessity of blackness for the feathers of a crow, so from the bird being a crow it follows that its feathers are black, and the necessity here is one-sided, because it does not hold that whenever black color is found it is the color of crow's feathers.
2 - And what has a rational basis from the reality of the system of causes and effects, its example: if the temperature drops below zero, water freezes, so the necessity observed in this example is causal necessity, because the drop in temperature was the cause of water freezing, so freezing follows from the drop, and this freezing has a rational basis from the reality of the system of causes and effects.
3 - And what has a rational basis from pure rational necessity, its example:
• If a thing exists, it is certainly not non-existent, and if it is non-existent, it is certainly not existent.
• An integer is either even or odd.
• Two numbers are either equal or one is greater and the other lesser.
• Two bodies are either equal or one is larger and the other smaller.
So the necessity observed in these examples is necessity required by rational necessity.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies logical necessity (luzum) into three types. The author begins by stating that necessity is either coincidental (ittifaqi) without a rational basis, or it has a rational basis (muqtada 'aqli) from either the causal order of the world or from pure reason. The first type is exemplified by the blackness of a crow's feathers: being a crow necessarily entails black feathers, but blackness does not entail being a crow (one-way necessity). The second type is causal necessity, as when a drop in temperature causes water to freeze—here the necessity is rooted in the actual causal nexus. The third type is purely rational necessity, such as the law of non-contradiction (existence implies non-non-existence) or the exhaustive classification of integers into even and odd. These are necessities of reason itself, not dependent on empirical observation. The passage thus distinguishes three distinct sources of logical entailment.

Key Terms
لزوم
اتفاقي
له مقتض عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Logical necessity is classified into three types based on its source: coincidental (no rational basis), causal (based on cause-effect), and purely rational (self-evident truths of reason).

The Modality of Necessity in a Proposition
Author defines the modality of necessity with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the modality of necessity in a categorical proposition and express it explicitly using the term 'darura'.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition carries a modality — necessity, impossibility, or possibility.

This passage introduces the concept of modality (jiha) in categorical propositions, specifically rational necessity. You will learn how to recognize when the relation between subject and predicate is necessary, and how to make that modality explicit. Understanding modality prevents confusion between the truth of a proposition and the strength of that truth.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is affirmative (e.g., 'Allah is living') or negative (e.g., 'No god but Allah').
The modality applies regardless of polarity.
2
Determine the necessity of the relation
Ask: Can the relation between subject and predicate be otherwise? If reason says no, it is necessary. For example, 'Allah is living' cannot be false.
Necessity means the opposite is impossible.
3
Express the modality explicitly
Add the word 'bi al-darura' (necessarily) to the proposition. For affirmative: 'Allah is necessarily living'. For negative: 'Necessarily, no god but Allah'.
The modal term marks the force of the judgment.
4
Note the three modalities
The author lists three rational judgments: necessity, impossibility (a kind of necessity), and possibility. Here necessity includes both positive necessity and the impossibility of the opposite.
Impossibility is the modality of the negative side of necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۳)
الجهة في القضية
نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع في القضية - سواء أكانت موجبة أو سالبة - لا بد أن تكون على جهة الوجوب العقلي، أو على جهة الإمكان العقلي.
أ - فجهة الوجوب العقلي هي التي يحكم فيها العقل بضرورة كون النسبة في القضية موجبة، أو بضرورة كونها سالبة، فإيجاب النسبة أو سلبها عندئذ أمر حتمي.
الأمثلة :
١ - (الله حيّ) هذه قضية حملية موجبة، والنسبة فيها واجبة عقلاً، فالحكم فيها حكم ضروري الثبوت، ولا يمكن تخلفه.
فإذا صرّحنا في القضية بهذا المعنى فقلنا: (الله حي بالضرورة)، كان هذا التصريح بياناً لجهة القضية، ضمن واحد من أحكام العقل الثلاثة [الوجوب - الاستحالة - الإمكان (الجواز)].
والجهة في هذا المثال هي (وجوب كون الله حيّاً) المعبر عنه بلفظ (الضرورة).
٢ - (لا إله إلا الله) هذه قضية حملية سالبة، والنسبة السالبة فيها واجبة عقلاً، فالحكم فيها ضروري، لاستحالة وجود إله حق غير الله جل وعلا.
فإذا صرّحنا في القضية بهذا المعنى فقلنا: (بالضرورة لا إله إلا الله) كان هذا التصريح بياناً لجهة القضية، وهي (استحالة وجود إله بحق غير الله) المعبر عنه بلفظ (الضرورة).
(13) The Modality in the Proposition. The relation of the predicate to the subject in the proposition — whether affirmative or negative — must be with a modality of rational necessity or rational possibility. A) The modality of rational necessity is when reason judges the necessity of the relation being affirmative, or the necessity of it being negative, so affirming or negating the relation is then definite. Examples: 1) 'Allah is living' is an affirmative categorical proposition, and the relation in it is rationally necessary, so the judgment in it is a necessarily true judgment, and cannot be otherwise. So if we explicitly state this meaning in the proposition and say 'Allah is necessarily living', this explicit statement is a clarification of the modality of the proposition, within one of the three rational judgments [necessity - impossibility - possibility (permissibility)]. And the modality in this example is 'the necessity of Allah being living' expressed by the word 'necessity'. 2) 'There is no god but Allah' is a negative categorical proposition, and the negative relation in it is rationally necessary, so the judgment in it is necessary, due to the impossibility of the existence of a true god other than Allah, glorified and exalted. So if we explicitly state this meaning in the proposition and say 'necessarily, there is no god but Allah', this explicit statement is a clarification of the modality of the proposition, which is 'the impossibility of the existence of a true god other than Allah' expressed by the word 'necessity'.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines the modality (jiha) of a proposition, specifically rational necessity (wujub 'aqli). The author states that every categorical proposition has a modal qualification: either necessity, impossibility, or possibility. Here he focuses on necessity. Two theological examples illustrate: 'Allah is living' is necessarily true because the predicate 'living' is essential to Allah; adding the word 'necessarily' (bi al-darura) makes the modality explicit. The second example, 'There is no god but Allah', is a negative proposition whose truth is also necessary because any other god is impossible. Interestingly, the modal term 'necessity' can cover both positive necessity and negative necessity (impossibility of the opposite). This shows that modality is about the force of the judgment, not the polarity of the proposition.

وجوب عقلي
ضرورة

Rational necessity means the relation in a proposition must hold, and it can be expressed explicitly by adding 'bi al-darura'.

Definition of Modality (al-Jiha) and Its Two Types
Definition of modality and its division into necessity and possibility

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish rational necessity from rational possibility in categorical propositions and identify the modality of any given proposition from Islamic kalam or natural science.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition has a modality: either the predicate is necessary or possible for the subject.

In the Islamic logical tradition, every categorical proposition carries a modality (jiha) that indicates the nature of the connection between subject and predicate. This lesson explores the two fundamental modalities defined by the intellect: rational necessity (wujub 'aqli) and rational possibility (imkan 'aqli). Understanding modality is crucial for evaluating the strength of arguments in theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition's subject and predicate
Find the subject (the thing talked about) and the predicate (what is said about it) in the proposition.
2
Ask: Is the predicate necessary for the subject?
Check if the intellect (aql) compels that the predicate must belong to the subject (or must not belong). If the opposite is inconceivable, then the proposition is rationally necessary.
3
If not necessary, is it possible?
If the relation is not necessary and not impossible, then it is rationally possible. The intellect allows either affirmation or negation without contradiction.
4
Determine the modality from the examples
Use the examples given (like theological and mathematical ones for necessity, natural ones for possibility) to practice classification.
5
Check for permanence
If necessary, the modality implies perpetuity (dawam); if possible, it is contingent.

الجهة في القضية
نسبة المحمول إلى الموضوع في القضية - سواء أكانت موجبة أو سالبة - لا بد أن تكون على جهة الوجوب العقلي، أو على جهة الإمكان العقلي.
أ - فجهة الوجوب العقلي هي التي يحكم فيها العقل بضرورة كون النسبة في القضية موجبة، أو بضرورة كونها سالبة، فإيجاب النسبة أو سلبها عندئذ أمر حتمي.
الأمثلة :
١ - (الله حيّ) هذه قضية حملية موجبة، والنسبة فيها واجبة عقلاً، فالحكم فيها حكم ضروري الثبوت، ولا يمكن تخلفه. فإذا صرّحنا في القضية بهذا المعنى فقلنا: (الله حي بالضرورة)، كان هذا التصريح بياناً لجهة القضية، ضمن واحد من أحكام العقل الثلاثة [الوجوب - الاستحالة - الإمكان (الجواز)]. والجهة في هذا المثال هي (وجوب كون الله حيّاً) المعبر عنه بلفظ (الضرورة).
٢ - (لا إله إلا الله) هذه قضية حملية سالبة، والنسبة السالبة فيها واجبة عقلاً، فالحكم فيها ضروري، لاستحالة وجود إله حق غير الله جل وعلا. فإذا صرّحنا في القضية بهذا المعنى فقلنا: (بالضرورة لا إله إلا الله) كان هذا التصريح بياناً لجهة القضية، وهي (استحالة وجود إله بحق غير الله) المعبر عنه بلفظ (الضرورة).
وحين نصرح في القضية بجهتها تسمى القضية موجهة، ونمثل أيضاً للقضية الموجهة بالضرورة بالأمثلة التالية :
بالضرورة العدد الزوج ينقسم إلى متساويين صحيحين - بالضرورة كل الشيء أكبر من جزئه - بالضرورة لا ينقسم العدد الفرد إلى متساويين صحيحين - بالضرورة ليس جزء الشيء أكبر من كله - بالضرورة العدد إما زوج أو فرد - بالضرورة العددان إما متساويان أو متفاوتان - وهكذا .
وما كان ضرورياً (أي : واجب الوجود أو مستحيل الوجود) فالدوام ملازم له حتماً، فجهة القضية على هذا جهة الضرورة والدوام .
ب - وجهة الإمكان العقلي هي التي يحكم فيها العقل بأن النسبة في القضية ممكنة عقلاً، لا واجبة ولا مستحيلة، سواء أكانت القضية موجبة أو سالبة .
الأمثلة :
١ - (النار محرقة للأشياء القابلة للاحتراق هذه قضية حملية موجبة، والنسبة فيها ممكنة عقلاً ليست بواجبة، لأن اتصاف النار بالإحراق ليس أمراً يوجبه العقل، وإنما هو أمر اقتضاه نظام هذا الكون، ولا مانع لدى العقل من تغير هذا النظام، إذ ليس بين مادة النار وبين الإحراق ارتباط عقلي . فإذا صرحنا في القضية بهذا المعنى فقلنا : (بالإمكان النار محرقة للأشياء القابلة للاحتراق) كان هذا التصريح بياناً لجهة القضية ضمن واحد من أحكام العقل الثلاثة الوجوب - الاستحالة - الإمكان والجهة هنا هي الإمكان .
٢ - (الثلج لا يحرق الأشياء القابلة للاحتراق) هذه قضية حملية سالبة، والنسبة فيها ممكنة عقلاً ليست بواجبة، لأن اتصاف الثلج بعدم الإحراق ليس أمراً يوجبه العقل، وإنما هو أمر اقتضاه نظام الكون، ولا مانع عند العقل من إمكان تغير هذا النظام حتى يكون الثلج محرقاً، إذ ليس بين الثلج وعدم الاحتراق به ارتباط عقلي .
Modality in the proposition. The relation of the predicate to the subject in a proposition - whether affirmative or negative - must be either in a mode of rational necessity or in a mode of rational possibility. A - The mode of rational necessity is that in which the intellect judges the necessity of the relation in the proposition being affirmative, or the necessity of it being negative, so affirming the relation or negating it is then an inevitable matter. Examples: 1 - 'Allah is living' is an affirmative categorical proposition, and the relation in it is rationally necessary, for the judgment is a necessary affirmation and cannot be otherwise. If we explicitly state this meaning in the proposition and say 'Allah is living by necessity,' this explicit statement is an explanation of the modality of the proposition, within one of the three judgments of the intellect [necessity - impossibility - possibility (permissibility)]. The modality in this example is (the necessity of Allah being living) expressed by the word (necessity). 2 - 'There is no god but Allah' is a negative categorical proposition, and the negative relation in it is rationally necessary, for the judgment is necessary due to the impossibility of the existence of a true god other than Allah, may He be exalted. If we explicitly state this meaning in the proposition and say 'By necessity, there is no god but Allah,' this explicit statement is an explanation of the modality of the proposition, which is (the impossibility of the existence of a true god other than Allah) expressed by the word (necessity). And when we explicitly state its modality in the proposition, the proposition is called modal. We also give the following examples of the modal proposition with necessity: By necessity, an even number is divisible into two equal integers - By necessity, every whole is greater than its part - By necessity, an odd number is not divisible into two equal integers - By necessity, a part of a thing is not greater than its whole - By necessity, a number is either even or odd - By necessity, two numbers are either equal or unequal - and so on. And whatever is necessary (i.e., necessary of existence or impossible of existence) is necessarily accompanied by perpetuity, so the modality of the proposition on this basis is the modality of necessity and perpetuity. B - The mode of rational possibility is that in which the intellect judges that the relation in the proposition is rationally possible, neither necessary nor impossible, whether the proposition is affirmative or negative. Examples: 1 - 'Fire is burning of combustible things' is an affirmative categorical proposition, and the relation in it is rationally possible, not necessary, because the attribution of burning to fire is not something the intellect necessitates; rather it is a matter demanded by the system of this universe, and there is no impediment in the intellect to changing this system, since there is no rational connection between the substance of fire and burning. If we explicitly state this meaning in the proposition and say 'Possibly fire is burning of combustible things,' this explicit statement is an explanation of the modality of the proposition within one of the three judgments of the intellect: necessity - impossibility - possibility. The modality here is possibility. 2 - 'Snow does not burn combustible things' is a negative categorical proposition, and the relation in it is rationally possible, not necessary, because the attribution of not burning to snow is not something the intellect necessitates; rather it is a matter demanded by the system of the universe, and there is no impediment in the intellect to the possibility of changing this system such that snow becomes burning, since there is no rational connection between snow and not being burned by it.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of modality (al-jiha) in categorical propositions and divides it into two fundamental types: rational necessity (al-wujub al-'aqli) and rational possibility (al-imkan al-'aqli). The author begins by stating that every categorical proposition—whether affirmative or negative—must have its predicate-subject relation qualified by one of these two modalities. This is a foundational concept in modal logic, which classifies propositions not just by truth value but by the mode of their truth. The first type, rational necessity, occurs when the intellect judges that the relation must be either affirmed or denied; it cannot be otherwise. The author gives two clear examples from Islamic creed: 'Allah is living' is necessarily true, and 'There is no god but Allah' is necessarily true (the negation of any other god). In both cases, the necessity is intrinsic to the meaning. The author notes that when we explicitly state the modality, the proposition is called 'modal' (muwajjaha). He then lists additional examples of necessary propositions, such as mathematical truths like 'every whole is greater than its part,' highlighting that these are rationally necessary. Importantly, the author identifies that what is necessary (whether affirmative or negative) is permanent—it cannot change. Therefore, necessity implies perpetuity (al-dawam). The second type, rational possibility, occurs when the intellect judges that the relation could be either way; it is neither necessary nor impossible. The author gives the example of 'fire burns combustible things'—this is not rationally necessary because we can conceive of a world where fire does not burn; the intellect does not compel the connection. Similarly, 'snow does not burn' is possible, not necessary, because the non-burning is due to the current natural order, not a logical necessity. The passage thus delineates the two modes, using examples from theology, mathematics, and natural science to illustrate the distinction.

الوجوب العقلي
الإمكان العقلي
الضرورة

Key Takeaway
Modality in categorical propositions is either rational necessity (the intellect compels the relation) or rational possibility (the intellect allows both affirmation and negation), and necessity implies permanence.

Understanding Modal Propositions: Possibility vs Necessity
Author defines modal propositions and the opposition of possibility and necessity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a modal proposition and distinguish between possibility (imkān) and necessity (ḍarūra) in the context of Islamic logic.

Core Idea
A modal proposition explicitly states its modality—whether its content is necessary, possible, or impossible.

Modal propositions form the backbone of rigorous reasoning in Islamic theology and jurisprudence. When we say 'by possibility, snow does not burn,' we are not making a simple assertion—we are specifying the mode of truth. This lesson unpacks the fundamental opposition between possibility and necessity, which underlies all modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Recognize explicit modality markers
Look for phrases like 'بالإمكان' (by possibility) or 'بالضرورة' (by necessity) in the Arabic. These markers transform a simple proposition into a modal one.
2
Differentiate possibility from necessity
Possibility (imkān) means a proposition is neither necessary nor impossible—it could be true or false. Necessity (ḍarūra) includes both necessary existence (wujūb al-wujūd) and necessary non-existence (wajūb al-‘adam, i.e., impossibility).
The passage emphasises that 'ḍarūra' covers both existence and non-existence modalities.
3
Understand the scope of intellectual possibility
What is intellectually possible (mumkin ‘aqlan) may or may not occur in actuality. The passage explains that actual existence can be perpetual or non-perpetual, and conditional or unconditional.
4
Define 'modal proposition' (mūjaha)
A proposition is called 'modal' (موجهة) when it explicitly states its modality according to one of the divisions. For example, 'Allah is necessarily existent' is modal because it includes the modality of necessity.
This is a descriptive definition (rasm) based on the presence of explicit qualification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإذا صرحنا في القضية بهذا المعنى فقلنا: (بالإمكان لا يحرق الثلج الأشياء القابلة للاحتراق) كان هذا التصريح بياناً لجهة الإمكان في القضية. والإمكان يقابل الضرورة، والضرورة إما أن تكون بوجوب الوجود، أو بوجوب العدم، وهو ما يعبر عنه بالاستحالة. ثم إن ما هو ممكن عقلاً قد يكون غير واقع بالفعل، وقد يكون واقعاً بالفعل، والواقع بالفعل إما أن يكون على جهة الدوام أو لا على جهة الدوام، وإما أن يكون مشروطاً بشرط أو غير مشروط بشرط. والضرورة كذلك إما أن تكون مشروطة بشرط، أو غير مشروطة بشرط. وبناء على هذه الاحتمالات تتعدد أقسام توجيه القضية، فمنها الضروريات، ومنها الدوائم، ومنها المطلقات، ومنها الممكنات، ومنها المشروطات، وحين يصرح في القضية بصفتها وفق أحد هذه الأقسام تسمى موجهة. وتتشعب أقسام الموجهات، ولذلك فإننا نقتصر على هذا البيان حولها اكتفاء بالتعريف بأصل الموضوع، وهو يرجع كما عرفنا إلى الضرورة والإمكان وأقسامهما.
If we explicitly state this meaning in the proposition and say: 'By possibility, snow does not burn combustible things,' then this explicit statement is an explanation of the modality of possibility in the proposition. Possibility is opposite to necessity. Necessity is either by necessity of existence or by necessity of non-existence, which is expressed as impossibility. Then, what is intellectually possible may not exist in actuality, or may exist in actuality. The actually existent is either perpetual or not perpetual, and either conditional upon a condition or unconditional. Likewise, necessity is either conditional upon a condition or unconditional. Based on these possibilities, the divisions of propositional modality multiply. Among them are the necessary propositions, the perpetual ones, the absolute ones, the possible ones, and the conditional ones. When the quality of the proposition is explicitly stated according to one of these divisions, it is called a modal proposition. The divisions of modal propositions branch out, so we restrict ourselves to this explanation, contenting ourselves with introducing the origin of the subject, which, as we have learned, goes back to necessity and possibility and their divisions.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage defines modal propositions (al-mūjaha) and their primary classifications. The author begins with an example: stating 'by possibility, snow does not burn combustible things' clarifies the modality of possibility (imkān) in the proposition. Possibility is contrasted with necessity (ḍarūra), which encompasses both necessary existence (wajūb al-wujūd) and necessary non-existence (wajūb al-‘adam), i.e., impossibility (istiḥāla). The author then explains that what is intellectually possible (mumkin ‘aqlan) may or may not occur in actuality. If it does occur, it may be perpetual (dā’im) or non-perpetual, and conditional (mashrūṭ) or unconditional. Similarly, necessity may be conditional or unconditional. From these possibilities, the divisions of propositional modality arise: necessary (ḍarūriyyāt), perpetual (dā’imāt), absolute (muṭlaqāt), possible (mumkināt), and conditional (mashrūṭāt). When a proposition explicitly states its modality according to one of these divisions, it is called a modal proposition (mūjaha). The author concludes by noting that the divisions branch out further, but they suffice with this basic introduction, as the core concept reduces to necessity and possibility and their subdivisions.

الضرورة
الموجهة

Key Takeaway
Modal propositions are defined by the explicit mention of their modality, and the primary modal opposition is between possibility (non-necessary) and necessity (including impossibility).

The Five Divisions of Modal Propositions: Necessary, Perpetual, Absolute, Possible, and Conditional
Author enumerates five main divisions of modal propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and describe the five main divisions of modal propositions as derived from the categories of possibility and necessity.

Core Idea
Modalities branch from necessity and possibility into five specific types: necessary, perpetual, absolute, possible, and conditional.

Building on the foundation of possibility and necessity, the author enumerates five concrete modal divisions that appear in logical discourse. Understanding these five types is essential for analysing propositions in Islamic theological arguments, where precise modality is crucial. This lesson walks you through how each division emerges from the binary splits of possibility and necessity.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the splits within possibility
Intellectual possibility (imkān) can either be actual (realized in existence) or non-actual. If actual, it can be perpetual (dā’im) or non-perpetual. Also, it can be conditional (mashrūṭ) or unconditional. These splits generate several modal types.
The passage says: 'الواقع بالفعل إما أن يكون على جهة الدوام أو لا على جهة الدوام، وإما أن يكون مشروطاً بشرط أو غير مشروط بشرط.'
2
Recognize the splits within necessity
Necessity (ḍarūra) likewise can be conditional (mashrūṭ) or unconditional. This gives rise to the 'conditional necessary' type.
3
Derive the five modal divisions
The author lists five divisions that arise from these possibilities: الضروريات (necessary), الدوائم (perpetual), المطلقات (absolute), الممكنات (possible), المشروطات (conditional). Note that 'absolute' (mutlaq) likely corresponds to possibility without further qualification.
The passage: 'فمنها الضروريات، ومنها الدوائم، ومنها المطلقات، ومنها الممكنات، ومنها المشروطات.'
4
Identify a proposition as belonging to one of these types
Given a modal proposition, match its explicit modal term to one of the five. For example, 'necessarily' → necessary; 'perpetually' → perpetual; 'absolutely' → absolute; 'possibly' → possible; 'conditionally' → conditional.
The passage concludes that when a proposition explicitly states its quality according to these divisions, it is called a modal proposition.

فإذا صرحنا في القضية بهذا المعنى فقلنا: (بالإمكان لا يحرق الثلج الأشياء القابلة للاحتراق) كان هذا التصريح بياناً لجهة الإمكان في القضية. والإمكان يقابل الضرورة، والضرورة إما أن تكون بوجوب الوجود، أو بوجوب العدم، وهو ما يعبر عنه بالاستحالة. ثم إن ما هو ممكن عقلاً قد يكون غير واقع بالفعل، وقد يكون واقعاً بالفعل، والواقع بالفعل إما أن يكون على جهة الدوام أو لا على جهة الدوام، وإما أن يكون مشروطاً بشرط أو غير مشروط بشرط. والضرورة كذلك إما أن تكون مشروطة بشرط، أو غير مشروطة بشرط. وبناء على هذه الاحتمالات تتعدد أقسام توجيه القضية، فمنها الضروريات، ومنها الدوائم، ومنها المطلقات، ومنها الممكنات، ومنها المشروطات، وحين يصرح في القضية بصفتها وفق أحد هذه الأقسام تسمى موجهة. وتتشعب أقسام الموجهات، ولذلك فإننا نقتصر على هذا البيان حولها اكتفاء بالتعريف بأصل الموضوع، وهو يرجع كما عرفنا إلى الضرورة والإمكان وأقسامهما.
If we explicitly state this meaning in the proposition and say: 'By possibility, snow does not burn combustible things,' then this explicit statement is an explanation of the modality of possibility in the proposition. Possibility is opposite to necessity. Necessity is either by necessity of existence or by necessity of non-existence, which is expressed as impossibility. Then, what is intellectually possible may not exist in actuality, or may exist in actuality. The actually existent is either perpetual or not perpetual, and either conditional upon a condition or unconditional. Likewise, necessity is either conditional upon a condition or unconditional. Based on these possibilities, the divisions of propositional modality multiply. Among them are the necessary propositions, the perpetual ones, the absolute ones, the possible ones, and the conditional ones. When the quality of the proposition is explicitly stated according to one of these divisions, it is called a modal proposition. The divisions of modal propositions branch out, so we restrict ourselves to this explanation, contenting ourselves with introducing the origin of the subject, which, as we have learned, goes back to necessity and possibility and their divisions.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage defines modal propositions (al-mūjaha) and their primary classifications. The author begins with an example: stating 'by possibility, snow does not burn combustible things' clarifies the modality of possibility (imkān) in the proposition. Possibility is contrasted with necessity (ḍarūra), which encompasses both necessary existence (wajūb al-wujūd) and necessary non-existence (wajūb al-‘adam), i.e., impossibility (istiḥāla). The author then explains that what is intellectually possible (mumkin ‘aqlan) may or may not occur in actuality. If it does occur, it may be perpetual (dā’im) or non-perpetual, and conditional (mashrūṭ) or unconditional. Similarly, necessity may be conditional or unconditional. From these possibilities, the divisions of propositional modality arise: necessary (ḍarūriyyāt), perpetual (dā’imāt), absolute (muṭlaqāt), possible (mumkināt), and conditional (mashrūṭāt). When a proposition explicitly states its modality according to one of these divisions, it is called a modal proposition (mūjaha). The author concludes by noting that the divisions branch out further, but they suffice with this basic introduction, as the core concept reduces to necessity and possibility and their subdivisions.

الدوائم
المطلقات
الممكنات
المشروطات

Key Takeaway
The five primary modal divisions—necessary, perpetual, absolute, possible, and conditional—arise from the binary splits of possibility and necessity and are the standard categories for modal propositions in Islamic logic.

Categorical Propositions and Their Opposites
Defining the four quantified propositions and their opposites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of categorical propositions (A, E, I, O) and produce their logical opposites by changing quantity and/or quality.

Core Idea
Every quantified proposition has three opposites formed by toggling quantity and quality.

In this lesson, we learn the four quantified propositions that form the foundation of logical reasoning. The author defines each type and shows how changing its quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative) yields its logical opposites. This is the basis for the square of opposition.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the four proposition types
Identify universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), particular negative (O). Study the examples: 'Every human is an animal' (A), 'No human is a horse' (E), 'Some human is a writer' (I), 'Some human is not white-skinned' (O).
These are often labeled A, E, I, O from the Latin 'affirmo' and 'nego'.
2
Understand how to change quality and quantity
Quality is affirmative or negative; quantity is universal or particular. To generate opposites, you may change the quality to its opposite, the quantity to its opposite, or both. The subject and predicate remain the same.
3
List the opposites for each type
For a universal affirmative (A), the opposites are: universal negative (E) by changing quality, particular affirmative (I) by changing quantity, and particular negative (O) by changing both. Practice this for each of the four types.
The text shows this for A, E, I, O with examples.
4
Note exceptions for singular propositions
Singular propositions, like 'Damascus is the capital of the Umayyads', have no quantity change possible. Their only opposite is the negation (singular negative). They are excluded from the square.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية: جزئية موجبة (بعض الإنسان حيوان). الثالثة: جزئية سالبة (بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان). وهذه القضايا نتجت من التقابل في الكم والكيف، وذلك بتبديل الكيف بمقابله، أو بتبديل الكم بمقابله، أو بتبديل الكيف والكم معاً بما يقابل كلاً منهما. ولدى تبديل الكيف والكم في القضايا بمقابلاتها مع المحافظة التامة على الموضوع والمحمول وكل قيودهما وشروطهما ومتعلقاتهما تنتج التقابلات التاليات: ١ - شخصية موجبة - تقابلها شخصية سالبة مثل: - دمشق عاصمة الأمويين - دمشق ليست عاصمة للأمويين ٢ - كلية موجبة، مثل: (كل إنسان حيوان). تقابلها - كلية سالبة = لا شيء من الإنسان بحيوان جزئية موجبة = بعض الإنسان حيوان جزئية سالبة = بعض الإنسان ليس بحيوان ٣ - كلية سالبة، مثل: (لا شيء من الإنسان بفرس). تقابلها - كلية موجبة = كل إنسان فرس جزئية موجبة = بعض الإنسان فرس جزئية سالبة = بعض الإنسان ليس بفرس ٤ - جزئية موجبة مثل: (بعض الإنسان كاتب). تقابلها - كلية موجبة = كل إنسان كاتب كلية سالبة = لا شيء من الإنسان بكاتب جزئية سالبة = بعض الإنسان ليس بكاتب ٥ - جزئية سالبة، مثل: (بعض الإنسان ليس بأبيض البشرة). تقابلها - كلية موجبة = كل إنسان أبيض البشرة كلية سالبة = لا شيء من الإنسان بأبيض البشرة جزئية موجبة = بعض الإنسان أبيض البشرة فإذا استعدنا إلى الذاكرة أن المهملة تلازم الجزئية علمنا أن المهملة تندرج فيها . ثم إذا تركنا الشخصية لأنها لا تقابل إلا بشخصية مثلها، إذ لا يجري التبديل فيها إلا في الكيف، ووضعنا المحصورات الأربع على زوايا شكل مربع رسم عليه قطراه نتجت معنا كل التقابلات الواقعة في الاحتمال بالنسبة إلى المحصورات على الوجه التالي : كلية موجبة ـــــــ
Second: particular affirmative (Some human is animal). Third: particular negative (Some human is not animal). These propositions result from opposition in quantity and quality, namely by changing the quality to its opposite, or by changing the quantity to its opposite, or by changing both quality and quantity to their opposite. And when changing the quality and quantity of propositions to their opposites while preserving the subject and predicate and all their restrictions, conditions, and attachments, the following opposites result: 1 - Singular affirmative - its opposite is singular negative, such as: - Damascus is the capital of the Umayyads - Damascus is not the capital of the Umayyads. 2 - Universal affirmative, e.g. (Every human is an animal). Its opposites: universal negative = No human is an animal; particular affirmative = Some human is an animal; particular negative = Some human is not an animal. 3 - Universal negative, e.g. (No human is a horse). Its opposites: universal affirmative = Every human is a horse; particular affirmative = Some human is a horse; particular negative = Some human is not a horse. 4 - Particular affirmative, e.g. (Some human is a writer). Then if we leave aside the singular because it has no opposite except a like singular, since change in it occurs only in quality, and we place the four quantified propositions at the corners of a square shape on which we draw its two diagonals, there result for us all the oppositions that occur in possibility concerning the quantified propositions in the following manner: universal affirmative ―――

It then shows how to construct the square of opposition. After listing the five types including singular, the author explains that indefinite propositions are treated as particular. Singular propositions are excluded from the square because they only have one opposite (its negation). The Square of Opposition
Constructing the square of opposition and its logical relations

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the square of opposition, label the four corners with the correct proposition types, and identify the relations of contrary, subcontrary, contradictory, and subalternation.

Core Idea
The square of opposition maps the logical relationships among the four quantified propositions in a single diagram.

Once we have the four proposition types, the author arranges them into a square with diagonals. This square immediately reveals all possible logical relations: contraries (cannot both be true), subcontraries (cannot both be false), contradictories (always opposite truth values), and subalternation (truth flows downward). This lesson walks through the square.

Guided Path
1
Place the four propositions at the corners
Imagine a square with universal affirmative (A) at top left, universal negative (E) at top right, particular affirmative (I) at bottom left, particular negative (O) at bottom right.
This arrangement follows the standard Aristotelian square.
2
Identify the top line: contraries
A and E are contraries: they cannot both be true (e.g., 'Every human is an animal' and 'No human is an animal' cannot both be true), but they may both be false.
3
Identify the bottom line: subcontraries
I and O are subcontraries: they cannot both be false (e.g., 'Some human is a writer' and 'Some human is not a writer' cannot both be false), but they may both be true.
4
Identify the diagonals: contradictories
A and O are contradictories: they always have opposite truth values. Similarly, E and I are contradictories. If one is true, the other is false, and vice versa.
The text says 'by changing both quality and quantity' yields the contradictory.
5
Identify the vertical sides: subalternation
If the universal (A or E) is true, the corresponding particular (I or O) is also true. Truth flows downward from the universal to the particular. The converse does not hold.
This is why the universal implies the particular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Understanding Contradiction in Singular Propositions
Defines contradiction for singular propositions with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify contradictory pairs of singular propositions and explain why they satisfy the conditions of contradiction.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires identical subject/predicate but opposite quality; truth of one forces falsehood of the other.

In this passage, the author presents the logical definition of contradiction (tanāquḍ) and applies it to singular propositions. By understanding this definition, you will be able to recognize contradictory pairs and avoid confusion with other logical relations like contrariety. This tool is essential for constructing sound arguments in Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions of contradiction
From the definition, list the core requirements: (1) truth of one forces falsehood of the other, (2) falsehood of one forces truth of the other, (3) they cannot be both true or both false.
Memorize these as the standard definition.
2
Examine the given example
Look at the pair 'Four is half of eight' and 'Four is not half of eight'. Verify that they have the same subject ('Four') and same predicate ('half of eight'), and differ only in quality (affirmative vs negative).
Singular propositions have no quantifier, so identity is straightforward.
3
Test the conditions against the example
Check logically: if it is true that four is half of eight, then necessarily it is false that four is not half of eight. They cannot be both true; they cannot be both false. Hence they are contradictory.
This is a simple mathematical truth, but the logical structure applies to any singular proposition.
4
Generalize the pattern
For any singular proposition P and its negation not-P, the pair is contradictory provided the subject and predicate are identical. This is the foundation of the law of non-contradiction.
Apply this to Islamic examples like 'The Quran is revealed' and 'The Quran is not revealed'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التناقض في القضايا : معناه أن صدق إحدى القضيتين ينقض احتمال صدق الأخرى ويجعلها كاذبة حتماً، وأن كذب إحداهما ينقض احتمال كذب الأخرى، ويجعلها صادقة حتماً، فهما على هذا لا يصدقان معاً بحال من الأحوال، ولا يكذبان معاً بحال من الأحوال.
التناقض في القضايا المخصوصة (الشخصية)
أمثلة :
القضية مقابلتها
أ ـ الأربعة نصف الثمانية يقابلها الأربعة ليست نصف الثمانية
هاتان قضيتان اتحد موضوعهما ومحمولهما من كل الوجوه، ولكن اختلف الكيف فيهما إيجاباً وسلباً.
فالأولى موجبة ـ ومقابلتها سالبة .
ونلاحظ بداهة أنه إذا كانت القضية الأولى صادقة حتماً فالقضية الثانية كاذبة حتماً، فلا يمكن أن تكونا صادقتين معاً، ولا يمكن أن تكونا كاذبتين معاً، فبينهما تناقض، إذ صدق إحداهما ينقض حتماً صدق الأخرى، ويجعلها كاذبة .
Contradiction in propositions: its meaning is that the truth of one of the two propositions negates the possibility of the truth of the other and makes it necessarily false, and the falsehood of one negates the possibility of the falsehood of the other and makes it necessarily true. Thus they are not both true in any condition, nor are they both false in any condition.
Contradiction in particular (singular) propositions.
Examples:
Proposition            Its opposite
A: Four is half of eight — its opposite: Four is not half of eight.
These are two propositions whose subject and predicate are identical in all respects, but the quality differs between them in affirmation and negation.
Thus the first is affirmative and its opposite is negative.
We note as obvious that if the first proposition is necessarily true, then the second proposition is necessarily false; so they cannot both be true together, nor can they both be false together. Hence between them is contradiction, for the truth of one necessarily negates the truth of the other and makes it false.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines the logical relation of contradiction (tanāquḍ) and illustrates it with singular propositions. The author begins by giving a general definition: contradiction means that if one proposition is true, the other must be false, and if one is false, the other must be true. Therefore they can never be both true or both false. The author then narrows the discussion to singular (personal) propositions and provides an example. The example uses the proposition 'Four is half of eight' and its opposite 'Four is not half of eight'. These two propositions share the same subject and predicate but differ in quality: one is affirmative, the other negative. The author points out that it is obvious that if the first is true, the second is false, and vice versa, thus they fulfill the conditions of contradiction. This passage establishes the foundation for understanding how contradiction operates in logic, particularly for singular propositions where there is no quantification involved.

تناقض
قضية شخصية
كيف

Key Takeaway
Contradiction is a fundamental logical relation where two singular propositions with identical subject and predicate but opposite quality cannot be both true or both false.

Definition of Propositional Contradiction
Defines propositional contradiction and shows how to form contradictories for singular propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define propositional contradiction and determine whether two given propositions satisfy the conditions for contradiction.

Core Idea
Contradiction is when two propositions can never be both true nor both false.

Contradiction is the strongest form of logical opposition. In this passage, the author gives a precise definition and shows why certain pairs of propositions cannot share a truth value. Understanding this definition is essential for evaluating arguments and debates in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of contradiction
Read the definition: the truth of one proposition negates the possibility of truth of the other, making it necessarily false; and the falsehood of one makes the other necessarily true.
2
Identify the two conditions
Two conditions must hold: they cannot be both true simultaneously, and they cannot be both false simultaneously.
3
Apply the conditions to a pair
Take the example 'Four is half of eight' and 'Four is not half of eight'. Check that if the first is true, the second is false, and if the first is false, the second is true. Neither can they be both true nor both false.
4
Distinguish contradiction from other relations
Contradiction is stricter than contrariety (where both cannot be true but can be false) or subcontrariety (both can be true but cannot both be false).
This distinction is not in the passage but helps clarify.

Source Text

التناقض

فالأولى موجبة ـ ومقابلتها سالبة .

هاتان قضيتان اتحد موضوعهما ومحمولهما من كل الوجوه، ولكن اختلف الكيف فيهما إيجاباً وسلباً.
فالأولى سالبة - ومقابلتها موجبة.

وبهذا نلاحظ أن نقيض القضية الشخصية (المخصوصة) هو مثلها تماماً مع تبديل الكيف فيها، فإن كان موجباً وضع بدله سالب في القضية المقابلة، وإن كان سالباً وضع بدله موجب في القضية المقابلة، هذا مع المحافظة التامة على حَدَّي القضية (الموضوع والمحمول) بكل قيودهما وشروطهما ومتعلقاتهما.
فنقيض المخصوصة (الشخصية) الموجبة - شخصية سالبة.
(أ) - هو - (ب) نقيضها (أ) - هو ليس - (ب).
ونقيض المخصوصة (الشخصية) السالبة - مخصوصة موجبة.
(أ) - هو ليس - (ب) نقيضها (أ) - هو - (ب).
ولذلك كفر الذين قالوا لمحمد ﷺ: (لست مرسلاً) مخصوصة سالبة.
في مقابل قول الله جلّ وعلا: (محمد رسول الله) مخصوصة موجبة.
القضية مقابلتها
ف: لست يا محمد مرسلاً تقابلها محمد رسول الله
Contradiction. Contradiction in propositions: its meaning is that the truth of one of the two propositions negates the possibility of truth of the other and makes it necessarily false, and that the falsehood of one negates the possibility of falsehood of the other and makes it necessarily true. Thus they are not both true under any circumstances, nor both false under any circumstances. Contradiction in particular (singular) propositions. Examples: Proposition vs. its counterpart: A) 'Four is half of eight' is countered by 'Four is not half of eight'. These are two propositions whose subject and predicate are identical in all respects, but the quality differs between them in affirmation and negation. The first is affirmative, and its counterpart is negative. And we intuitively observe that if the first proposition is necessarily true, then the second proposition is necessarily false. It is impossible for both to be true together, and impossible for both to be false together. Thus there is contradiction between them, for the truth of one necessarily negates the truth of the other and makes it false. Proposition vs. its counterpart: B) 'God has no partner' is countered by 'God has a partner'. These are two propositions whose subject and predicate are identical in all respects, but the quality differs between them in affirmation and negation. The first is negative, and its counterpart is affirmative. And here also we intuitively observe that if the first is necessarily true, then the second proposition is necessarily false, because it is impossible for both to be true together, and impossible for both to be false together, due to the contradiction between them. That is, the truth of one necessarily negates the truth of the other and makes it false. By this we observe that the contradictory of a personal (particular) proposition is exactly like it with a change in its quality. If it is affirmative, a negative is put in its place in the counterpart proposition; if it is negative, an affirmative is put in its place in the counterpart proposition. This is while fully preserving the two limits (subject and predicate) of the proposition with all their qualifications, conditions, and attachments. So the contradictory of an affirmative particular (singular) is a negative singular. (A) is (B) — its contradictory is (A) is not (B). And the contradictory of a negative particular (singular) is an affirmative particular. (A) is not (B) — its contradictory is (A) is (B). For that reason, those who said to Muhammad (PBUH) 'You are not a messenger' (a negative singular) disbelieved, in contrast to the word of God Most High: 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God' (an affirmative singular). Proposition vs. its counterpart: So: 'You, O Muhammad, are not a messenger' is countered by 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God'.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of contradiction (al-tanaquḍ) between propositions and then applies it specifically to singular propositions (al-qadāyā al-mukhaṣṣaṣa). The author begins by giving the general definition: two propositions are contradictory if the truth of one necessarily implies the falsehood of the other, and the falsehood of one necessarily implies the truth of the other. Consequently, they can never be both true nor both false. The author then moves to singular propositions, which are propositions about an individual subject. Two examples are given. The first is a mathematical example: 'Four is half of eight' and its contradictory 'Four is not half of eight'. These share the same subject and predicate in every respect except quality (kayf): one is affirmative, the other negative. The author notes the intuitive observation that they cannot be both true or both false. The second example is theological: 'God has no partner' (negative) and 'God has a partner' (affirmative). Again, they differ only in quality. From these examples, the author derives the rule: the contradictory of a singular proposition is exactly the same proposition with the quality reversed—affirmative becomes negative, negative becomes affirmative—while keeping all other conditions (quantifiers, time, etc.) unchanged. The rule is then expressed symbolically: the contradictory of 'A is B' is 'A is not B', and vice versa. Finally, the author gives a sacred example: the statement of the disbelievers 'You (Muhammad) are not a messenger' is a negative singular proposition whose contradictory is the Quranic verse 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God' (affirmative singular). This illustrates how the rule applies to real theological disputations.

Key Terms
التناقض
القضية المخصوصة (الشخصية)
الكيف
النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for two propositions to be contradictory?
A) They cannot be both true, but can be both false.
B) They cannot be both true or both false.
C) They can be both true or both false.
D) They must have different subjects.

Key Takeaway
Contradiction is defined by the impossibility of both truth and both falsehood; it is the strictest logical opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the pair of propositions from the passage: 'God has no partner' and 'God has a partner'. Verify step by step that they satisfy the definition of contradiction (truth of one implies falsehood of the other, and they cannot be both true or both false).
?

Propositions

Forming Contradictories of Singular Propositions
Defines propositional contradiction and shows how to form contradictories for singular propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to form the contradictory of any singular categorical proposition by changing its quality while preserving all other elements.

Core Idea
To contradict a singular proposition, simply flip its quality (affirmative ↔ negative).

The passage establishes a simple rule: the contradictory of a singular proposition is identical except for its quality. This rule is essential for constructing valid arguments and refutations in Islamic logic, especially when dealing with individual subjects like prophets or God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the singular proposition
A singular proposition has an individual subject (e.g., 'Zayd', 'Muhammad', 'God'). Identify its subject and predicate.
2
Determine its quality
Is the proposition affirmative (e.g., 'Zayd is standing') or negative (e.g., 'Zayd is not standing')?
3
Flip the quality
If affirmative, make it negative; if negative, make it affirmative. Keep the subject and predicate exactly the same.
Do not change tense, quantifiers, or conditions.
4
Verify the pair is contradictory
Check that the original and the flipped proposition satisfy the definition of contradiction: one must be true and the other false.
